I moved after him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hang it all!--stop!\xe2\x80\x99 I cried.\r\n\r\nHe must have made pretty good travelling, because, before I had a foot\r\nin the hall, I heard the front door slam, and, when I reached the\r\nstreet, intent on calling him back, neither to the right nor to the\r\nleft was there a sign of him to be seen.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XIII.\r\n THE PICTURE\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I wonder what that nice-looking beggar really means, and who he\r\nhappens to be?\xe2\x80\x99 That was what I said to myself when I returned to the\r\nlaboratory. \xe2\x80\x98If it is true that, now and again, Providence does write a\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s character on his face, then there can\xe2\x80\x99t be the slightest shred of\r\na doubt that a curious one\xe2\x80\x99s been written on his. I wonder what his\r\nconnection has been with the Apostle,--or if it\xe2\x80\x99s only part of his game\r\nof bluff.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI strode up and down,--for the moment my interest in the experiments I\r\nwas conducting had waned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If it was all bluff I never saw a better piece of acting,--and yet\r\nwhat sort of finger can such a precisian as St Paul have in such a pie?\r\nThe fellow seemed to squirm at the mere mention of the\r\nrising-hope-of-the-Radicals\xe2\x80\x99 name. Can the objection be political? Let\r\nme consider,--what has Lessingham done which could offend the religious\r\nor patriotic susceptibilities of the most fanatical of Orientals?\r\nPolitically, I can recall nothing. Foreign affairs, as a rule, he has\r\ncarefully eschewed. If he has offended--and if he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t the seeming\r\nwas uncommonly good!--the cause will have to be sought upon some other\r\ntrack. But, then, what track?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe more I strove to puzzle it out, the greater the puzzlement grew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Absurd!--The rascal has had no more connection with St Paul than St\r\nPeter. The probability is that he\xe2\x80\x99s a crackpot; and if he isn\xe2\x80\x99t, he has\r\nsome little game on foot--in close association with the hunt of the\r\noof-bird!--which he tried to work off on me, but couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. As for--for\r\nMarjorie--my Marjorie!--only she isn\xe2\x80\x99t mine, confound it!--if I had had\r\nmy senses about me, I should have broken his head in several places for\r\ndaring to allow her name to pass his lips,--the unbaptised\r\nMohammedan!--Now to return to the chase of splendid murder!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI snatched up my mask--one of the most ingenious inventions, by the\r\nway, of recent years; if the armies of the future wear my mask they\r\nwill defy my weapon!--and was about to re-adjust it in its place, when\r\nsomeone knocked at the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who\xe2\x80\x99s there?--Come in!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was Edwards. He looked round him as if surprised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I beg your pardon, sir,--I thought you were engaged. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nthat--that gentleman had gone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He went up the chimney, as all that kind of gentlemen do.--Why the\r\ndeuce did you let him in when I told you not to?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Really, sir, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I gave him your message, and--he looked\r\nat me, and--that is all I remember till I found myself standing in\r\nthis room.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHad it not been Edwards I might have suspected him of having had his\r\npalm well greased,--but, in his case, I knew better. It was as I\r\nthought,--my visitor was a mesmerist of the first class; he had\r\nactually played some of his tricks, in broad daylight, on my servant,\r\nat my own front door,--a man worth studying. Edwards continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There is someone else, sir, who wishes to see you,--Mr Lessingham.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Lessingham!\xe2\x80\x99 At that moment the juxtaposition seemed odd, though I\r\ndaresay it was so rather in appearance than in reality. \xe2\x80\x98Show him in.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nPresently in came Paul.\r\n\r\nI am free to confess,--I have owned it before!--that, in a sense, I\r\nadmire that man,--so long as he does not presume to thrust himself into\r\na certain position. He possesses physical qualities which please my\r\neye--speaking as a mere biologist. I like the suggestion conveyed by\r\nhis every pose, his every movement, of a tenacious hold on life,--of\r\nreserve force, of a repository of bone and gristle on which he can fall\r\nback at pleasure. The fellow\xe2\x80\x99s lithe and active; not hasty, yet agile;\r\nclean built, well hung,--the sort of man who might be relied upon to\r\nmake a good recovery. You might beat him in a sprint,--mental or\r\nphysical--though to do that you would have to be spry!--but in a\r\nstaying race he would see you out. I do not know that he is exactly the\r\nkind of man whom I would trust,--unless I knew that he was on the\r\njob,--which knowledge, in his case, would be uncommonly hard to attain.\r\nHe is too calm; too self-contained; with the knack of looking all round\r\nhim even in moments of extremest peril,--and for whatever he does he\r\nhas a good excuse. He has the reputation, both in the House and out of\r\nit, of being a man of iron nerve,--and with some reason; yet I am not\r\nso sure. Unless I read him wrongly his is one of those individualities\r\nwhich, confronted by certain eventualities, collapse,--to rise, the\r\nmoment of trial having passed, like Phoenix from her ashes. However it\r\nmight be with his adherents, he would show no trace of his disaster.\r\n\r\nAnd this was the man whom Marjorie loved. Well, she could show some\r\ncause. He was a man of position,--destined, probably, to rise much\r\nhigher; a man of parts,--with capacity to make the most of them; not\r\nill-looking; with agreeable manners,--when he chose; and he came within\r\nthe lady\xe2\x80\x99s definition of a gentleman, \xe2\x80\x98he always did the right thing,\r\nat the right time, in the right way.\xe2\x80\x99 And yet--! Well, I take it that\r\nwe are all cads, and that we most of us are prigs; for mercy\xe2\x80\x99s sake do\r\nnot let us all give ourselves away.\r\n\r\nHe was dressed as a gentleman should be dressed,--black frock coat,\r\nblack vest, dark grey trousers, stand-up collar, smartly-tied bow,\r\ngloves of the proper shade, neatly brushed hair, and a smile, which if\r\nwas not childlike, at any rate was bland.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am not disturbing you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not at all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sure?--I never enter a place like this, where a man is matching\r\nhimself with nature, to wrest from her her secrets, without feeling\r\nthat I am crossing the threshold of the unknown. The last time I was in\r\nthis room was just after you had taken out the final patents for your\r\nSystem of Telegraphy at Sea, which the Admiralty\r\npurchased,--wisely--What is it, now?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Death.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No?--really?--what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you are a member of the next government, you will possibly learn; I\r\nmay offer them the refusal of a new wrinkle in the art of murder.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I see,--a new projectile.--How long is this race to continue between\r\nattack and defence?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Until the sun grows cold.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99ll be no defence,--nothing to defend.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe looked at me with his calm, grave eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The theory of the Age of Ice towards which we are advancing is not a\r\ncheerful one.\xe2\x80\x99 He began to finger a glass retort which lay upon a\r\ntable. \xe2\x80\x98By the way, it was very good of you to give me a look in last\r\nnight. I am afraid you thought me peremptory,--I have come to\r\napologise.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I thought you peremptory; I thought you--queer.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99 He glanced at me with that expressionless look upon his face\r\nwhich he could summon at will, and which is at the bottom of the\r\nsuperstition about his iron nerve. \xe2\x80\x98I was worried, and not well.\r\nBesides, one doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care to be burgled, even by a maniac.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Was he a maniac?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did you see him?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Very clearly.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In the street.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How close were you to him?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Closer than I am to you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you were so close to him as that. Did you try to\r\nstop him?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Easier said than done,--he was off at such a rate.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did you see how he was dressed,--or, rather, undressed?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I did.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In nothing but a cloak on such a night. Who but a lunatic would have\r\nattempted burglary in such a costume?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did he take anything?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Absolutely nothing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It seems to have been a curious episode.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe moved his eyebrows,--according to members of the House the only\r\ngesture in which he has been known to indulge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We become accustomed to curious episodes. Oblige me by not mentioning\r\nit to anyone,--to anyone.\xe2\x80\x99 He repeated the last two words, as if to\r\ngive them emphasis. I wondered if he was thinking of Marjorie. \xe2\x80\x98I am\r\ncommunicating with the police. Until they move I don\xe2\x80\x99t want it to get\r\ninto the papers,--or to be talked about. It\xe2\x80\x99s a worry,--you understand?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI nodded. He changed the theme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This that you\xe2\x80\x99re engaged upon,--is it a projectile or a weapon?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you are a member of the next government you will possibly know; if\r\nyou aren\xe2\x80\x99t you possibly won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I suppose you have to keep this sort of thing secret?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I do. It seems that matters of much less moment you wish to keep\r\nsecret.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You mean that business of last night? If a trifle of that sort gets\r\ninto the papers, or gets talked about,--which is the same thing!--you\r\nhave no notion how we are pestered. It becomes an almost unbearable\r\nnuisance. Jones the Unknown can commit murder with less inconvenience\r\nto himself than Jones the Notorious can have his pocket picked,--there\r\nis not so much exaggeration in that as there sounds.--Good-bye,--thanks\r\nfor your promise.\xe2\x80\x99 I had given him no promise, but that was by the way.\r\nHe turned as to go,--then stopped. \xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s another thing,--I believe\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re a specialist on questions of ancient superstitions and extinct\r\nreligions.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am interested in such subjects, but I am not a specialist.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Can you tell me what were the exact tenets of the worshippers of Isis?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Neither I nor any man,--with scientific certainty. As you know, she\r\nhad a brother; the cult of Osiris and Isis was one and the same. What,\r\nprecisely, were its dogmas, or its practices, or anything about it,\r\nnone, now, can tell. The Papyri, hieroglyphics, and so on, which remain\r\nare very far from being exhaustive, and our knowledge of those which do\r\nremain, is still less so.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I suppose that the marvels which are told of it are purely legendary?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To what marvels do you particularly refer?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Weren\xe2\x80\x99t supernatural powers attributed to the priests of Isis?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Broadly speaking, at that time, supernatural powers were attributed to\r\nall the priests of all the creeds.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I see.\xe2\x80\x99 Presently he continued. \xe2\x80\x98I presume that her cult is long since\r\nextinct,--that none of the worshippers of Isis exist to-day.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI hesitated,--I was wondering why he had hit on such a subject; if he\r\nreally had a reason, or if he was merely asking questions as a cover\r\nfor something else,--you see, I knew my Paul.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That is not so sure.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe looked at me with that passionless, yet searching glance of his.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You think that she still is worshipped?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think it possible, even probable, that, here and there, in\r\nAfrica--Africa is a large order!--homage is paid to Isis, quite in the\r\ngood old way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you know that as a fact?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Excuse me, but do you know it as a fact?--Are you aware that you are\r\ntreating me as if I was on the witness stand?--Have you any special\r\npurpose in making these inquiries?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In a kind of a way I have. I have recently come across rather a\r\ncurious story; I am trying to get to the bottom of it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What is the story?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am afraid that at present I am not at liberty to tell it you; when I\r\nam I will. You will find it interesting,--as an instance of a singular\r\nsurvival.--Didn\xe2\x80\x99t the followers of Isis believe in transmigration?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Some of them,--no doubt.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What did they understand by transmigration?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Transmigration.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,--but of the soul or of the body?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How do you mean?--transmigration is transmigration. Are you driving at\r\nsomething in particular? If you\xe2\x80\x99ll tell me fairly and squarely what it\r\nis I\xe2\x80\x99ll do my best to give you the information you require; as it is,\r\nyour questions are a bit perplexing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter,--as you say, \xe2\x80\x9ctransmigration is\r\ntransmigration.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 I was eyeing him keenly; I seemed to detect in his\r\nmanner an odd reluctance to enlarge on the subject he himself had\r\nstarted. He continued to trifle with the retort upon the table. \xe2\x80\x98Hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthe followers of Isis a--what shall I say?--a sacred emblem?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hadn\xe2\x80\x99t they an especial regard for some sort of a--wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it some sort\r\nof a--beetle?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You mean _Scarabaeus sacer_,--according to Latreille, _Scarabaeus\r\nEgyptiorum_? Undoubtedly,--the scarab was venerated throughout\r\nEgypt,--indeed, speaking generally, most things that had life, for\r\ninstance, cats; as you know, Orisis continued among men in the figure\r\nof Apis, the bull.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Weren\xe2\x80\x99t the priests of Isis--or some of them--supposed to assume,\r\nafter death, the form of a--scarabaeus?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I never heard of it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Are you sure?--think!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to answer such a question positively, offhand, but I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t, on the spur of the moment, recall any supposition of the kind.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t laugh at me--I\xe2\x80\x99m not a lunatic!--but I understand that recent\r\nresearches have shown that even in some of the most astounding of the\r\nancient legends there was a substratum of fact. Is it absolutely\r\ncertain that there could be no shred of truth in such a belief?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In what belief?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In the belief that a priest of Isis--or anyone--assumed after death\r\nthe form of a scarabaeus?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It seems to me, Lessingham, that you have lately come across some\r\nuncommonly interesting data, of a kind, too, which it is your bounden\r\nduty to give to the world,--or, at any rate, to that portion of the\r\nworld which is represented by me. Come,--tell us all about it!--what\r\nare you afraid of?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am afraid of nothing,--and some day you shall be told,--but not now.\r\nAt present, answer my question.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then repeat your question,--clearly.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is it absolutely certain that there could be no foundation of truth in\r\nthe belief that a priest of Isis--or anyone--assumed after death the\r\nform of a beetle?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know no more than the man in the moon,--how the dickens should I?\r\nSuch a belief may have been symbolical. Christians believe that after\r\ndeath the body takes the shape of worms--and so, in a sense, it\r\ndoes,--and, sometimes, eels.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That is not what I mean.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Listen. If a person, of whose veracity there could not be a vestige of\r\na doubt, assured you that he had seen such a transformation actually\r\ntake place, could it conceivably be explained on natural grounds?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Seen a priest of Isis assume the form of a beetle?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Or a follower of Isis?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Before, or after death?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe hesitated. I had seldom seen him wear such an appearance of\r\ninterest,--to be frank, I was keenly interested too!--but, on a sudden\r\nthere came into his eyes a glint of something that was almost terror.\r\nWhen he spoke, it was with the most unwonted awkwardness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In--in the very act of dying.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In the very act of dying?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If--he had seen a follower of Isis in--the very act of dying,\r\nassume--the form of a--a beetle, on any conceivable grounds would such\r\na transformation be susceptible of a natural explanation?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI stared,--as who would not? Such an extraordinary question was\r\nrendered more extraordinary by coming from such a man,--yet I was\r\nalmost beginning to suspect that there was something behind it more\r\nextraordinary still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Look here, Lessingham, I can see you\xe2\x80\x99ve a capital tale to tell,--so\r\ntell it, man! Unless I\xe2\x80\x99m mistaken, it\xe2\x80\x99s not the kind of tale in which\r\nordinary scruples can have any part or parcel,--anyhow, it\xe2\x80\x99s hardly\r\nfair of you to set my curiosity all agog, and then to leave it\r\nunappeased.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe eyed me steadily, the appearance of interest fading more and more,\r\nuntil, presently, his face assumed its wonted expressionless\r\nmask,--somehow I was conscious that what he had seen in my face was not\r\naltogether to his liking. His voice was once more bland and\r\nself-contained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I perceive you are of opinion that I have been told a taradiddle. I\r\nsuppose I have.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But what is the tarradiddle?--don\xe2\x80\x99t you see I\xe2\x80\x99m burning?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Unfortunately, Atherton, I am on my honour. Until I have permission to\r\nunloose it, my tongue is tied.\xe2\x80\x99 He picked up his hat and umbrella from\r\nwhere he had placed them on the table. Holding them in his left hand,\r\nhe advanced to me with his right outstretched. \xe2\x80\x98It is very good of you\r\nto suffer my continued interruption; I know, to my sorrow, what such\r\ninterruptions mean,--believe me, I am not ungrateful. What is this?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nOn the shelf, within a foot or so of where I stood, was a sheet of\r\npaper,--the size and shape of half a sheet of post note. At this he\r\nstooped to glance. As he did so, something surprising occurred. On the\r\ninstant a look came on to his face which, literally, transfigured him.\r\nHis hat and umbrella fell from his grasp on to the floor. He retreated,\r\ngibbering, his hands held out as if to ward something off from him,\r\nuntil he reached the wall on the other side of the room. A more amazing\r\nspectacle than he presented I never saw.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Lessingham!\xe2\x80\x99 I exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s wrong with you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMy first impression was that he was struck by a fit of\r\nepilepsy,--though anyone less like an epileptic subject it would be\r\nhard to find. In my bewilderment I looked round to see what could be\r\nthe immediate cause. My eye fell upon the sheet of paper. I stared at\r\nit with considerable surprise. I had not noticed it there previously, I\r\nhad not put it there,--where had it come from? The curious thing was\r\nthat, on it, produced apparently by some process of photogravure, was\r\nan illustration of a species of beetle with which I felt that I ought\r\nto be acquainted, and yet was not. It was of a dull golden green; the\r\ncolour was so well brought out,--even to the extent of seeming to\r\nscintillate, and the whole thing was so dexterously done that the\r\ncreature seemed alive. The semblance of reality was, indeed, so vivid\r\nthat it needed a second glance to be assured that it was a mere trick\r\nof the reproducer. Its presence there was odd,--after what we had been\r\ntalking about it might seem to need explanation; but it was absurd to\r\nsuppose that that alone could have had such an effect on a man like\r\nLessingham.\r\n\r\nWith the thing in my hand, I crossed to where he was,--pressing his\r\nback against the wall, he had shrunk lower inch by inch till he was\r\nactually crouching on his haunches.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Lessingham!--come, man, what\xe2\x80\x99s wrong with you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTaking him by the shoulder, I shook him with some vigour. My touch had\r\non him the effect of seeming to wake him out of a dream, of restoring\r\nhim to consciousness as against the nightmare horrors with which he was\r\nstruggling. He gazed up at me with that look of cunning on his face\r\nwhich one associates with abject terror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Atherton?--Is it you?--It\xe2\x80\x99s all right,--quite right.--I\xe2\x80\x99m well,--very\r\nwell.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs he spoke, he slowly drew himself up, till he was standing erect.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then, in that case, all I can say is that you have a queer way of\r\nbeing very well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe put his hand up to his mouth, as if to hide the trembling of his\r\nlips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s the pressure of overwork,--I\xe2\x80\x99ve had one or two attacks like\r\nthis,--but it\xe2\x80\x99s nothing, only--a local lesion.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI observed him keenly; to my thinking there was something about him\r\nwhich was very odd indeed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Only a local lesion!--If you take my strongly-urged advice you\xe2\x80\x99ll get\r\na medical opinion without delay,--if you haven\xe2\x80\x99t been wise enough to\r\nhave done so already.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to-day;--at once; but I know it\xe2\x80\x99s only mental overstrain.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re sure it\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to do with this?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI held out in front of him the photogravure of the beetle. As I did so\r\nhe backed away from me, shrieking, trembling as with palsy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Take it away! take it away!\xe2\x80\x99 he screamed.\r\n\r\nI stared at him, for some seconds, astonished into speechlessness. Then\r\nI found my tongue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Lessingham!--It\xe2\x80\x99s only a picture!--Are you stark mad?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe persisted in his ejaculations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Take it away! take it away!--Tear it up!--Burn it!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHis agitation was so unnatural,--from whatever cause it arose!--that,\r\nfearing the recurrence of the attack from which he had just recovered,\r\nI did as he bade me. I tore the sheet of paper into quarters, and,\r\nstriking a match, set fire to each separate piece. He watched the\r\nprocess of incineration as if fascinated. When it was concluded, and\r\nnothing but ashes remained, he gave a gasp of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Lessingham,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98you\xe2\x80\x99re either mad already, or you\xe2\x80\x99re going\r\nmad,--which is it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think it\xe2\x80\x99s neither. I believe I am as sane as you. It\xe2\x80\x99s--it\xe2\x80\x99s that\r\nstory of which I was speaking; it--it seems curious, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you\r\nall about it--some day. As I observed, I think you will find it an\r\ninteresting instance of a singular survival.\xe2\x80\x99 He made an obvious effort\r\nto become more like his usual self. \xe2\x80\x98It is extremely unfortunate,\r\nAtherton, that I should have troubled you with such a display of\r\nweakness,--especially as I am able to offer you so scant an\r\nexplanation. One thing I would ask of you,--to observe strict\r\nconfidence. What has taken place has been between ourselves. I am in\r\nyour hands, but you are my friend, I know I can rely on you not to\r\nspeak of it to anyone,--and, in particular, not to breathe a hint of it\r\nto Miss Lindon.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, in particular, not to Miss Lindon?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Can you not guess?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI hunched my shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If what I guess is what you mean is not that a cause the more why\r\nsilence would be unfair to her?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is for me to speak, if for anyone. I shall not fail to do what\r\nshould be done.--Give me your promise that you will not hint a word to\r\nher of what you have so unfortunately seen?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI gave him the promise he required.\r\n\r\n * * * * * * *\r\n\r\nThere was no more work for me that day. The Apostle, his divagations,\r\nhis example of the coleoptera, his Arabian friend,--these things were\r\nas microbes which, acting on a system already predisposed for their\r\nreception, produced high fever; I was in a fever,--of unrest. Brain in\r\na whirl!--Marjorie, Paul, Isis, beetle, mesmerism, in delirious jumble.\r\nLove\xe2\x80\x99s upsetting!--in itself a sufficiently severe disease; but when\r\ncomplications intervene, suggestive of mystery and novelties, so that\r\nyou do not know if you are moving in an atmosphere of dreams or of\r\nfrozen facts,--if, then, your temperature does not rise, like that\r\nrocket of M. Verne\xe2\x80\x99s,--which reached the moon, then you are a freak of\r\nan entirely genuine kind, and if the surgeons do not preserve you, and\r\nplace you on view, in pickle, they ought to, for the sake of historical\r\ndoubters, for no one will believe that there ever was a man like you,\r\nunless you yourself are somewhere around to prove them Thomases.\r\n\r\nMyself,--I am not that kind of man. When I get warm I grow heated, and\r\nwhen I am heated there is likely to be a variety show of a gaudy kind.\r\nWhen Paul had gone I tried to think things out, and if I had kept on\r\ntrying something would have happened--so I went on the river instead.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XIV.\r\n THE DUCHESS\xe2\x80\x99 BALL\r\n\r\nThat night was the Duchess of Datchet\xe2\x80\x99s ball--the first person I saw\r\nas I entered the dancing-room was Dora Grayling.\r\n\r\nI went straight up to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Miss Grayling, I behaved very badly to you last night. I have come to\r\nmake to you my apologies,--to sue for your forgiveness!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My forgiveness?\xe2\x80\x99 Her head went back,--she has a pretty bird-like trick\r\nof cocking it a little on one side. \xe2\x80\x98You were not well. Are you better?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Quite.--You forgive me? Then grant me plenary absolution by giving me\r\na dance for the one I lost last night.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe rose. A man came up,--a stranger to me; she\xe2\x80\x99s one of the best\r\nhunted women in England,--there\xe2\x80\x99s a million with her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This is my dance, Miss Grayling.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe looked at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You must excuse me. I am afraid I have made a mistake. I had forgotten\r\nthat I was already engaged.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI had not thought her capable of it. She took my arm, and away we went,\r\nand left him staring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s he who\xe2\x80\x99s the sufferer now,\xe2\x80\x99 I whispered, as we went round,--she\r\ncan waltz!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You think so? It was I last night,--I did not mean, if I could help\r\nit, to suffer again. To me a dance with you means something.\xe2\x80\x99 She went\r\nall red,--adding, as an afterthought, \xe2\x80\x98Nowadays so few men really\r\ndance. I expect it\xe2\x80\x99s because you dance so well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWe danced the waltz right through, then we went to an impromptu shelter\r\nwhich had been rigged up on a balcony. And we talked. There\xe2\x80\x99s something\r\nsympathetic about Miss Grayling which leads one to talk about one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nself,--before I was half aware of it I was telling her of all my plans\r\nand projects,--actually telling her of my latest notion which,\r\nultimately, was to result in the destruction of whole armies as by a\r\nflash of lightning. She took an amount of interest in it which was\r\nsurprising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What really stands in the way of things of this sort is not theory but\r\npractice,--one can prove one\xe2\x80\x99s facts on paper, or on a small scale in a\r\nroom; what is wanted is proof on a large scale, by actual experiment.\r\nIf, for instance, I could take my plant to one of the forests of South\r\nAmerica, where there is plenty of animal life but no human, I could\r\ndemonstrate the soundness of my position then and there.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Think of the money it would cost.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought I was a friend of yours.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I had hoped you were.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then why don\xe2\x80\x99t you let me help you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Help me?--How?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98By letting you have the money for your South American experiment;--it\r\nwould be an investment on which I should expect to receive good\r\ninterest.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI fidgeted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is very good of you, Miss Grayling, to talk like that.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe became quite frigid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Please don\xe2\x80\x99t be absurd!--I perceive quite clearly that you are\r\nsnubbing me, and that you are trying to do it as delicately as you know\r\nhow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Miss Grayling!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I understand that it was an impertinence on my part to volunteer\r\nassistance which was unasked; you have made that sufficiently plain.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I assure you--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pray don\xe2\x80\x99t. Of course, if it had been Miss Lindon it would have been\r\ndifferent; she would at least have received a civil answer. But we are\r\nnot all Miss Lindon.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI was aghast. The outburst was so uncalled for,--I had not the faintest\r\nnotion what I had said or done to cause it; she was in such a\r\nsurprising passion--and it suited her!--I thought I had never seen her\r\nlook prettier,--I could do nothing else but stare. So she went\r\non,--with just as little reason.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here is someone coming to claim this dance,--I can\xe2\x80\x99t throw all my\r\npartners over. Have I offended you so irremediably that it will be\r\nimpossible for you to dance with me again?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Miss Grayling!--I shall be only too delighted.\xe2\x80\x99 She handed me her\r\ncard. \xe2\x80\x98Which may I have?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98For your own sake you had better place it as far off as you possibly\r\ncan.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They all seem taken.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter; strike off any name you please, anywhere and put\r\nyour own instead.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was giving me an almost embarrassingly free hand. I booked myself\r\nfor the next waltz but two,--who it was who would have to give way to\r\nme I did not trouble to inquire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Atherton!--Is that you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was,--it was also she. It was Marjorie! And so soon as I saw her I\r\nknew that there was only one woman in the world for me,--the mere sight\r\nof her sent the blood tingling through my veins. Turning to her\r\nattendant cavalier, she dismissed him with a bow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is there an empty chair?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe seated herself in the one Miss Grayling had just vacated. I sat\r\ndown beside her. She glanced at me, laughter in her eyes. I was all in\r\na stupid tremblement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You remember that last night I told you that I might require your\r\nfriendly services in diplomatic intervention?\xe2\x80\x99 I nodded,--I felt that\r\nthe allusion was unfair. \xe2\x80\x98Well, the occasion\xe2\x80\x99s come,--or, at least,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s very near.\xe2\x80\x99 She was still,--and I said nothing to help her. \xe2\x80\x98You\r\nknow how unreasonable papa can be.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI did,--never a more pig-headed man in England than Geoffrey\r\nLindon,--or, in a sense, a duller. But, just then, I was not prepared\r\nto admit it to his child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You know what an absurd objection he has to--Paul.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was an appreciative hesitation before she uttered the fellow\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nChristian name,--when it came it was with an accent of tenderness which\r\nstung me like a gadfly. To speak to me--of all men,--of the fellow in\r\nsuch a tone was--like a woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Has Mr Lindon no notion of how things stand between you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Except what he suspects. That is just where you are to come in, papa\r\nthinks so much of you--I want you to sound Paul\xe2\x80\x99s praises in his\r\near--to prepare him for what must come.\xe2\x80\x99 Was ever rejected lover\r\nburdened with such a task? Its enormity kept me still. \xe2\x80\x98Sydney, you\r\nhave always been my friend,--my truest, dearest friend. When I was a\r\nlittle girl you used to come between papa and me, to shield me from his\r\nwrath. Now that I am a big girl I want you to be on my side once more,\r\nand to shield me still.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHer voice softened. She laid her hand upon my arm. How, under her\r\ntouch, I burned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand what cause there has been for secrecy,--why\r\nshould there have been any secrecy from the first?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It was Paul\xe2\x80\x99s wish that papa should not be told.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is Mr Lessingham ashamed of you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sydney!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Or does he fear your father?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are unkind. You know perfectly well that papa has been prejudiced\r\nagainst him all along, you know that his political position is just now\r\none of the greatest difficulty, that every nerve and muscle is kept on\r\nthe continual strain, that it is in the highest degree essential that\r\nfurther complications of every and any sort should be avoided. He is\r\nquite aware that his suit will not be approved of by papa, and he\r\nsimply wishes that nothing shall be said about it till the end of the\r\nsession,--that is all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I see! Mr Lessingham is cautious even in love-making,--politician\r\nfirst, and lover afterwards.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well!--why not?--would you have him injure the cause he has at heart\r\nfor want of a little patience?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It depends what cause it is he has at heart.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What is the matter with you?--why do you speak to me like that?--it is\r\nnot like you at all.\xe2\x80\x99 She looked at me shrewdly, with flashing eyes.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is it possible that you are--jealous?--that you were in earnest in\r\nwhat you said last night?--I thought that was the sort of thing you\r\nsaid to every girl.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI would have given a great deal to take her in my arms, and press her\r\nto my bosom then and there,--to think that she should taunt me with\r\nhaving said to her the sort of thing I said to every girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What do you know of Mr Lessingham?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What all the world knows,--that history will be made by him.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There are kinds of history in the making of which one would not desire\r\nto be associated. What do you know of his private life,--it was to that\r\nthat I was referring.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Really,--you go too far. I know that he is one of the best, just as he\r\nis one of the greatest, of men; for me, that is sufficient.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you do know that, it is sufficient.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I do know it,--all the world knows it. Everyone with whom he comes in\r\ncontact is aware--must be aware, that he is incapable of a\r\ndishonourable thought or action.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Take my advice, don\xe2\x80\x99t appreciate any man too highly. In the book of\r\nevery man\xe2\x80\x99s life there is a page which he would wish to keep turned\r\ndown.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There is no such page in Paul\xe2\x80\x99s,--there may be in yours; I think that\r\nprobable.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you. I fear it is more than probable. I fear that, in my case,\r\nthe page may extend to several. There is nothing Apostolic about\r\nme,--not even the name.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sydney!--you are unendurable!--It is the more strange to hear you talk\r\nlike this since Paul regards you as his friend.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He flatters me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Are you not his friend?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is it not sufficient to be yours?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No,--who is against Paul is against me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That is hard.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How is it hard? Who is against the husband can hardly be for the\r\nwife,--when the husband and the wife are one.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But as yet you are not one.--Is my cause so hopeless?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What do you call your cause?--are you thinking of that nonsense you\r\nwere talking about last night?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe laughed!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You call it nonsense.--You ask for sympathy, and give--so much!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will give you all the sympathy you stand in need of,--I promise it!\r\nMy poor, dear Sydney!--don\xe2\x80\x99t be so absurd! Do you think that I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow you? You\xe2\x80\x99re the best of friends, and the worst of lovers,--as the\r\none, so true; so fickle as the other. To my certain knowledge, with how\r\nmany girls have you been in love,--and out again. It is true that, to\r\nthe best of my knowledge and belief, you have never been in love with\r\nme before,--but that\xe2\x80\x99s the merest accident. Believe me, my dear, dear\r\nSydney, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be in love with someone else to-morrow,--if you\xe2\x80\x99re not\r\nhalf-way there to-night. I confess, quite frankly, that, in that\r\ndirection, all the experience I have had of you has in nowise\r\nstrengthened my prophetic instinct. Cheer up!--one never knows!--Who is\r\nthis that\xe2\x80\x99s coming?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was Dora Grayling who was coming,--I went off with her without a\r\nword,--we were half-way through the dance before she spoke to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am sorry that I was cross to you just now, and--disagreeable.\r\nSomehow I always seem destined to show to you my most unpleasant side.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The blame was mine,--what sort of side do I show you? You are far\r\nkinder to me than I deserve,--now, and always.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That is what you say.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pardon me, it\xe2\x80\x99s true,--else how comes it that, at this time of day,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m without a friend in all the world?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You!--without a friend!--I never knew a man who had so many!--I never\r\nknew a person of whom so many men and women join in speaking well!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Miss Grayling!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As for never having done anything worth doing, think of what you have\r\ndone. Think of your discoveries, think of your inventions, think\r\nof--but never mind! The world knows you have done great things, and it\r\nconfidently looks to you to do still greater. You talk of being\r\nfriendless, and yet when I ask, as a favour--as a great favour!--to be\r\nallowed to do something to show my friendship, you--well, you snub me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I snub you!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You know you snubbed me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you really mean that you take an interest in--in my work?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You know I mean it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe turned to me, her face all glowing,--and I did know it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Will you come to my laboratory to-morrow morning?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Will I!--won\xe2\x80\x99t I!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98With your aunt?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, with my aunt.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you round, and tell you all there is to be told, and then if\r\nyou still think there\xe2\x80\x99s anything in it, I\xe2\x80\x99ll accept your offer about\r\nthat South American experiment,--that is, if it still holds good.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course it still holds good.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And we\xe2\x80\x99ll be partners.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Partners?--Yes,--we will be partners.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It will cost a terrific sum.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There are some things which never can cost too much.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s not my experience.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope it will be mine.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s a bargain?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98On my side, I promise you that it\xe2\x80\x99s a bargain.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhen I got outside the room I found that Percy Woodville was at my\r\nside. His round face was, in a manner of speaking, as long as my arm. He\r\ntook his glass out of his eye, and rubbed it with his handkerchief,--and\r\ndirectly he put it back he took it out and rubbed it again. I believe\r\nthat I never saw him in such a state of fluster,--and, when one speaks\r\nof Woodville, that means something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Atherton, I am in a devil of a stew.\xe2\x80\x99 He looked it. \xe2\x80\x98All of a\r\nheap!--I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a blow which I shall never get over!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then get under.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWoodville is one of those fellows who will insist on telling me their\r\nmost private matters,--even to what they owe their washerwomen for the\r\nruination of their shirts. Why, goodness alone can tell,--heaven knows\r\nI am not sympathetic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t be an idiot!--you don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I\xe2\x80\x99m suffering!--I\xe2\x80\x99m as nearly\r\nas possible stark mad.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s all right, old chap,--I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen you that way more than once\r\nbefore.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t talk like that,--you\xe2\x80\x99re not a perfect brute!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I bet you a shilling that I am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t torture me,--you\xe2\x80\x99re not. Atherton!\xe2\x80\x99 He seized me by the lapels\r\nof my coat, seeming half beside himself,--fortunately he had drawn me\r\ninto a recess, so that we were noticed by few observers. \xe2\x80\x98What do you\r\nthink has happened?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear chap, how on earth am I to know?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She\xe2\x80\x99s refused me!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Has she!--Well I never!--Buck up,--try some other address,--there are\r\nquite as good fish in the sea as ever came out of it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Atherton, you\xe2\x80\x99re a blackguard.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe had crumpled his handkerchief into a ball, and was actually bobbing\r\nat his eyes with it,--the idea of Percy Woodville being dissolved in\r\ntears was excruciatingly funny,--but, just then, I could hardly tell\r\nhim so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s not a doubt of it,--it\xe2\x80\x99s my way of being sympathetic. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nso down, man,--try her again!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s not the slightest use--I know it isn\xe2\x80\x99t--from the way she treated\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t be so sure--women often say what they mean least. Who\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nlady?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who?--Is there more women in the world than one for me, or has there\r\never been? You ask me who! What does the word mean to me but Marjorie\r\nLindon!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Marjorie Lindon?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI fancy that my jaw dropped open,--that, to use his own vernacular, I\r\nwas \xe2\x80\x98all of a heap.\xe2\x80\x99 I felt like it.\r\n\r\nI strode away--leaving him mazed--and all but ran into Marjorie\xe2\x80\x99s arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m just leaving. Will you see me to the carriage, Mr Atherton?\xe2\x80\x99 I saw\r\nher to the carriage. \xe2\x80\x98Are you off?--can I give you a lift?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you,--I am not thinking of being off.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m going to the House of Commons,--won\xe2\x80\x99t you come?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What are you going there for?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nDirectly she spoke of it I knew why she was going,--and she knew that I\r\nknew, as her words showed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are quite well aware of what the magnet is. You are not so\r\nignorant as not to know that the Agricultural Amendment Act is on\r\nto-night, and that Paul is to speak. I always try to be there when Paul\r\nis to speak, and I mean to always keep on trying.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He is a fortunate man.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed,--and again indeed. A man with such gifts as his is\r\ninadequately described as fortunate.--But I must be off. He expected to\r\nbe up before, but I heard from him a few minutes ago that there has\r\nbeen a delay, but that he will be up within half-an-hour.--Till our\r\nnext meeting.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs I returned into the house, in the hall I met Percy Woodville. He had\r\nhis hat on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where are you off to?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m off to the House.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To hear Paul Lessingham?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Damn Paul Lessingham!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98With all my heart!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s a division expected,--I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to go.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Someone else has gone to hear Paul Lessingham,--Marjorie Lindon.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No!--you don\xe2\x80\x99t say so!--by Jove!--I say, Atherton, I wish I could make\r\na speech,--I never can. When I\xe2\x80\x99m electioneering I have to have my\r\nspeeches written for me, and then I have to read \xe2\x80\x99em. But, by Jove, if\r\nI knew Miss Lindon was in the gallery, and if I knew anything about the\r\nthing, or could get someone to tell me something, hang me if I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nspeak,--I\xe2\x80\x99d show her I\xe2\x80\x99m not the fool she thinks I am!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Speak, Percy, speak!--you\xe2\x80\x99d knock \xe2\x80\x99em silly, sir!--I tell you what\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll do,--I\xe2\x80\x99ll come with you! I\xe2\x80\x99ll to the House as well!--Paul\r\nLessingham shall have an audience of three.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XV.\r\n MR LESSINGHAM SPEAKS\r\n\r\nThe House was full. Percy and I went upstairs,--to the gallery which\r\nis theoretically supposed to be reserved for what are called\r\n\xe2\x80\x98distinguished strangers,\xe2\x80\x99--those curious animals. Trumperton was up,\r\nhammering out those sentences which smell, not so much of the lamp as\r\nof the dunderhead. Nobody was listening,--except the men in the Press\r\nGallery; where is the brain of the House, and ninety per cent. of its\r\nwisdom.\r\n\r\nIt was not till Trumperton had finished that I discovered Lessingham.\r\nThe tedious ancient resumed his seat amidst a murmur of sounds which, I\r\nhave no doubt, some of the pressmen interpreted next day as \xe2\x80\x98loud and\r\ncontinued applause.\xe2\x80\x99 There was movement in the House, possibly\r\nexpressive of relief; a hum of voices; men came flocking in. Then, from\r\nthe Opposition benches, there rose a sound which was applause,--and I\r\nperceived that, on a cross bench close to the gangway, Paul Lessingham\r\nwas standing up bareheaded.\r\n\r\nI eyed him critically,--as a collector might eye a valuable specimen,\r\nor a pathologist a curious subject. During the last four and twenty\r\nhours my interest in him had grown apace. Just then, to me, he was the\r\nmost interesting man the world contained.\r\n\r\nWhen I remembered how I had seen him that same morning, a nerveless,\r\nterror-stricken wretch, grovelling, like some craven cur, upon the\r\nfloor, frightened, to the verge of imbecility, by a shadow, and less\r\nthan a shadow, I was confronted by two hypotheses. Either I had\r\nexaggerated his condition then, or I exaggerated his condition now. So\r\nfar as appearance went, it was incredible that this man could be that\r\none.\r\n\r\nI confess that my feeling rapidly became one of admiration. I love the\r\nfighter. I quickly recognised that here we had him in perfection. There\r\nwas no seeming about him then,--the man was to the manner born. To his\r\nfinger-tips a fighting man. I had never realised it so clearly before.\r\nHe was coolness itself. He had all his faculties under complete\r\ncommand. While never, for a moment, really exposing himself, he would\r\nbe swift in perceiving the slightest weakness in his opponents\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndefence, and, so soon as he saw it, like lightning, he would slip in a\r\ntelling blow. Though defeated, he would hardly be disgraced; and one\r\nmight easily believe that their very victories would be so expensive to\r\nhis assailants, that, in the end, they would actually conduce to his\r\nown triumph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hang me!\xe2\x80\x99 I told myself, \xe2\x80\x98if, after all, I am surprised if Marjorie\r\ndoes see something in him.\xe2\x80\x99 For I perceived how a clever and\r\nimaginative young woman, seeing him at his best, holding his own, like\r\na gallant knight, against overwhelming odds, in the lists in which he\r\nwas so much at home, might come to think of him as if he were always\r\nand only there, ignoring altogether the kind of man he was when the\r\njoust was finished.\r\n\r\nIt did me good to hear him, I do know that,--and I could easily imagine\r\nthe effect he had on one particular auditor who was in the Ladies\xe2\x80\x99\r\nCage. It was very far from being an \xe2\x80\x98oration\xe2\x80\x99 in the American sense; it\r\nhad little or nothing of the fire and fury of the French Tribune; it\r\nwas marked neither by the ponderosity nor the sentiment of the eloquent\r\nGerman; yet it was as satisfying as are the efforts of either of the\r\nthree, producing, without doubt, precisely the effect which the speaker\r\nintended. His voice was clear and calm, not exactly musical, yet\r\ndistinctly pleasant, and it was so managed that each word he uttered\r\nwas as audible to every person present as if it had been addressed\r\nparticularly to him. His sentences were short and crisp; the words\r\nwhich he used were not big ones, but they came from him with an\r\nagreeable ease; and he spoke just fast enough to keep one\xe2\x80\x99s interest\r\nalert without involving a strain on the attention.\r\n\r\nHe commenced by making, in the quietest and most courteous manner,\r\nsarcastic comments on the speeches and methods of Trumperton and his\r\nfriends which tickled the House amazingly. But he did not make the\r\nmistake of pushing his personalities too far. To a speaker of a certain\r\nsort nothing is easier than to sting to madness. If he likes, his every\r\nword is barbed. Wounds so given fester; they are not easily\r\nforgiven;--it is essential to a politician that he should have his\r\nfirmest friends among the fools; or his climbing days will soon be\r\nover. Soon his sarcasms were at an end. He began to exchange them for\r\nsweet-sounding phrases. He actually began to say pleasant things to his\r\nopponents; apparently to mean them. To put them in a good conceit with\r\nthemselves. He pointed out how much truth there was in what they said;\r\nand then, as if by accident, with what ease and at how little cost,\r\namendments might be made. He found their arguments, and took them for\r\nhis own, and flattered them, whether they would or would not, by\r\nshowing how firmly they were founded upon fact; and grafted other\r\narguments upon them, which seemed their natural sequelae; and\r\ntransformed them, and drove them hither and thither; and brought\r\nthem--their own arguments!--to a round, irrefragable conclusion, which\r\nwas diametrically the reverse of that to which they themselves had\r\nbrought them. And he did it all with an aptness, a readiness, a grace,\r\nwhich was incontestable. So that, when he sat down, he had performed\r\nthat most difficult of all feats, he had delivered what, in a House of\r\nCommons\xe2\x80\x99 sense, was a practical, statesmanlike speech, and yet one\r\nwhich left his hearers in an excellent humour.\r\n\r\nIt was a great success,--an immense success. A parliamentary triumph of\r\nalmost the highest order. Paul Lessingham had been coming on by leaps\r\nand bounds. When he resumed his seat, amidst applause which, this time,\r\nreally was applause, there were, probably, few who doubted that he was\r\ndestined to go still farther. How much farther it is true that time\r\nalone could tell; but, so far as appearances went, all the prizes,\r\nwhich are as the crown and climax of a statesman\xe2\x80\x99s career, were well\r\nwithin his reach.\r\n\r\nFor my part, I was delighted. I had enjoyed an intellectual\r\nexercise,--a species of enjoyment not so common as it might be. The\r\nApostle had almost persuaded me that the political game was one worth\r\nplaying, and that its triumphs were things to be desired. It is\r\nsomething, after all, to be able to appeal successfully to the passions\r\nand aspirations of your peers; to gain their plaudits; to prove your\r\nskill at the game you yourself have chosen; to be looked up to and\r\nadmired. And when a woman\xe2\x80\x99s eyes look down on you, and her ears drink\r\nin your every word, and her heart beats time with yours,--each man to\r\nhis own temperament, but when that woman is the woman whom you love, to\r\nknow that your triumph means her glory, and her gladness, to me that\r\nwould be the best part of it all.\r\n\r\nIn that hour,--the Apostle\xe2\x80\x99s hour!--I almost wished that I were a\r\npolitician too!\r\n\r\nThe division was over. The business of the night was practically done.\r\nI was back again in the lobby! The theme of conversation was the\r\nApostle\xe2\x80\x99s speech,--on every side they talked of it.\r\n\r\nSuddenly Marjorie was at my side. Her face was glowing. I never saw her\r\nlook more beautiful,--or happier. She seemed to be alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So you have come, after all!--Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it splendid?--wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it\r\nmagnificent? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it grand to have such great gifts, and to use them\r\nto such good purpose?--Speak, Sydney! Don\xe2\x80\x99t feign a coolness which is\r\nforeign to your nature!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI saw that she was hungry for me to praise the man whom she delighted\r\nto honour. But, somehow, her enthusiasm cooled mine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It was not a bad speech, of a kind.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of a kind!\xe2\x80\x99 How her eyes flashed fire! With what disdain she treated\r\nme! \xe2\x80\x98What do you mean by \xe2\x80\x9cof a kind?\xe2\x80\x9d My dear Sydney, are you not aware\r\nthat it is an attribute of small minds to attempt to belittle those\r\nwhich are greater? Even if you are conscious of inferiority, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nunwise to show it. Mr Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s was a great speech, of any kind;\r\nyour incapacity to recognise the fact simply reveals your lack of the\r\ncritical faculty.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is fortunate for Mr Lessingham that there is at least one person in\r\nwhom the critical faculty is so bountifully developed. Apparently, in\r\nyour judgment, he who discriminates is lost.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI thought she was going to burst into passion. But, instead, laughing,\r\nshe placed her hand upon my shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Poor Sydney!--I understand!--It is so sad!--Do you know you are like a\r\nlittle boy who, when he is beaten, declares that the victor has cheated\r\nhim. Never mind! as you grow older, you will learn better.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe stung me almost beyond bearing,--I cared not what I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You, unless I am mistaken, will learn better before you are older.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBefore I could have told her--if I had meant to tell; which I did\r\nnot--Lessingham came up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope I have not kept you waiting; I have been delayed longer than I\r\nexpected.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not at all,--though I am quite ready to get away; it\xe2\x80\x99s a little\r\ntiresome waiting here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThis with a mischievous glance towards me,--a glance which compelled\r\nLessingham to notice me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You do not often favour us.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t. I find better employment for my time.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are wrong. It\xe2\x80\x99s the cant of the day to underrate the House of\r\nCommons, and the work which it performs; don\xe2\x80\x99t you suffer yourself to\r\njoin in the chorus of the simpletons. Your time cannot be better\r\nemployed than in endeavouring to improve the body politic.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am obliged to you.--I hope you are feeling better than when I saw\r\nyou last.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA gleam came into his eyes, fading as quickly as it came. He showed no\r\nother sign of comprehension, surprise, or resentment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you.--I am very well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBut Marjorie perceived that I meant more than met the eye, and that\r\nwhat I meant was meant unpleasantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come,--let us be off. It is Mr Atherton to-night who is not well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe had just slipped her arm through Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s when her father\r\napproached. Old Lindon stared at her on the Apostle\xe2\x80\x99s arm, as if he\r\ncould hardly believe that it was she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought that you were at the Duchess\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So I have been, papa; and now I\xe2\x80\x99m here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here!\xe2\x80\x99 Old Lindon began to stutter and stammer, and to grow red in the\r\nface, as is his wont when at all excited. \xe2\x80\x98W--what do you mean by\r\nhere?--wh--where\xe2\x80\x99s the carriage?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where should it be, except waiting for me outside,--unless the horses\r\nhave run away.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I--I--I\xe2\x80\x99ll take you down to it. I--I don\xe2\x80\x99t approve of y--your\r\nw--w--waiting in a place like this.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you, papa, but Mr Lessingham is going to take me down.--I shall\r\nsee you afterwards.--Good-bye.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnything cooler than the way in which she walked off I do not think I\r\never saw. This is the age of feminine advancement. Young women think\r\nnothing of twisting their mothers round their fingers, let alone their\r\nfathers; but the fashion in which that young woman walked off, on the\r\nApostle\xe2\x80\x99s arm, and left her father standing there, was, in its way, a\r\nstudy.\r\n\r\nLindon seemed scarcely able to realise that the pair of them had gone.\r\nEven after they had disappeared in the crowd he stood staring after\r\nthem, growing redder and redder, till the veins stood out upon his\r\nface, and I thought that an apoplectic seizure threatened. Then, with a\r\ngasp, he turned to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Damned scoundrel!\xe2\x80\x99 I took it for granted that he alluded to the\r\ngentleman,--even though his following words hardly suggested it. \xe2\x80\x98Only\r\nthis morning I forbade her to have anything to do with him, and n--now\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s w--walked off with her! C--confounded adventurer! That\xe2\x80\x99s what he\r\nis, an adventurer, and before many hours have passed I\xe2\x80\x99ll take the\r\nliberty to tell him so!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nJamming his fists into his pockets, and puffing like a grampus in\r\ndistress, he took himself away,--and it was time he did, for his words\r\nwere as audible as they were pointed, and already people were wondering\r\nwhat the matter was. Woodville came up as Lindon was going,--just as\r\nsorely distressed as ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She went away with Lessingham,--did you see her?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course I saw her. When a man makes a speech like Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s any\r\ngirl would go away with him,--and be proud to. When you are endowed\r\nwith such great powers as he is, and use them for such lofty purposes,\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll walk away with you,--but, till then, never.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe was at his old trick of polishing his eyeglass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s bitter hard. When I knew that she was there, I\xe2\x80\x99d half a mind to\r\nmake a speech myself, upon my word I had, only I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to\r\nspeak about, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t speak anyhow,--how can a fellow speak when\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s shoved into the gallery?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As you say, how can he?--he can\xe2\x80\x99t stand on the railing and\r\nshout,--even with a friend holding him behind.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know I shall speak one day,--bound to; and then she won\xe2\x80\x99t be there.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99ll be better for you if she isn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Think so?--Perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99re right. I\xe2\x80\x99d be safe to make a mess of it, and\r\nthen, if she were to see me at it, it\xe2\x80\x99d be the devil! \xe2\x80\x99Pon my word,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been wishing, lately, I was clever.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe rubbed his nose with the rim of his eyeglass, looking the most\r\ncomically disconsolate figure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Put black care behind you, Percy!--buck up, my boy! The division\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nover--you are free--now we\xe2\x80\x99ll go \xe2\x80\x9con the fly.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnd we did \xe2\x80\x98go on the fly.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XVI.\r\n ATHERTON\xe2\x80\x99S MAGIC VAPOUR\r\n\r\nI bore him off to supper at the Helicon. All the way in the cab he was\r\ntrying to tell me the story of how he proposed to Marjorie,--and he was\r\nvery far from being through with it when we reached the club. There was\r\nthe usual crowd of supperites, but we got a little table to ourselves,\r\nin a corner of the room, and before anything was brought for us to eat\r\nhe was at it again. A good many of the people were pretty near to\r\nshouting, and as they seemed to be all speaking at once, and the band\r\nwas playing, and as the Helicon supper band is not piano, Percy did not\r\nhave it quite all to himself, but, considering the delicacy of his\r\nsubject, he talked as loudly as was decent,--getting more so as he went\r\non. But Percy is peculiar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how many times I\xe2\x80\x99ve tried to tell her,--over and over\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have you now?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, pretty near every time I met her,--but I never seemed to get\r\nquite to it, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How was that?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, just as I was going to say, \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Lindon, may I offer you the\r\ngift of my affection--\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Was that how you invariably intended to begin?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, not always--one time like that, another time another way. Fact\r\nis, I got off a little speech by heart, but I never got a chance to\r\nreel it off, so I made up my mind to just say anything.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And what did you say?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, nothing,--you see, I never got there. Just as I was feeling my\r\nway, she\xe2\x80\x99d ask me if I preferred big sleeves to little ones, or top\r\nhats to billycocks, or some nonsense of the kind.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Would she now?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,--of course I had to answer, and by the time I\xe2\x80\x99d answered the\r\nchance was lost.\xe2\x80\x99 Percy was polishing his eyeglass. \xe2\x80\x98I tried to get\r\nthere so many times, and she choked me off so often, that I can\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nthinking that she suspected what it was that I was after.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You think she did?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She must have done. Once I followed her down Piccadilly, and chivied\r\nher into a glove shop in the Burlington Arcade. I meant to propose to\r\nher in there,--I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had a wink of sleep all night through dreaming\r\nof her, and I was just about desperate.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And did you propose?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The girl behind the counter made me buy a dozen pairs of gloves\r\ninstead. They turned out to be three sizes too large for me when they\r\ncame home. I believe she thought I\xe2\x80\x99d gone to spoon the glove girl,--she\r\nwent out and left me there. That girl loaded me with all sorts of\r\nthings when she was gone,--I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get away. She held me with her\r\nblessed eye. I believe it was a glass one.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Miss Lindon\xe2\x80\x99s?--or the glove girl\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The glove girl\xe2\x80\x99s. She sent me home a whole cartload of green ties, and\r\ndeclared I\xe2\x80\x99d ordered them. I shall never forget that day. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never\r\nbeen up the Arcade since, and never mean to.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You gave Miss Lindon a wrong impression.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I was always giving her wrong impressions. Once she said\r\nthat she knew I was not a marrying man, that I was the sort of chap who\r\nnever would marry, because she saw it in my face.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Under the circumstances, that was trying.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bitter hard.\xe2\x80\x99 Percy sighed again. \xe2\x80\x98I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind if I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t so\r\ngone. I\xe2\x80\x99m not a fellow who does get gone, but when I do get gone, I get\r\nso beastly gone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I tell you what, Percy,--have a drink!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m a teetotaler,--you know I am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You talk of your heart being broken, and of your being a teetotaler in\r\nthe same breath,--if your heart were really broken you\xe2\x80\x99d throw\r\nteetotalism to the winds.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you think so,--why?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Because you would,--men whose hearts are broken always do,--you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nswallow a magnum at the least.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nPercy groaned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When I drink I\xe2\x80\x99m always ill,--but I\xe2\x80\x99ll have a try.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe had a try,--making a good beginning by emptying at a draught the\r\nglass which the waiter had just now filled. Then he relapsed into\r\nmelancholy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell me, Percy,--honest Indian!--do you really love her?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Love her?\xe2\x80\x99 His eyes grew round as saucers. \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you that I\r\nlove her?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know you tell me, but that sort of thing is easy telling. What does\r\nit make you feel like, this love you talk so much about?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Feel like?--Just anyhow,--and nohow. You should look inside me, and\r\nthen you\xe2\x80\x99d know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I see.--It\xe2\x80\x99s like that, is it?--Suppose she loved another man, what\r\nsort of feeling would you feel towards him?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Does she love another man?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I say, suppose.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I dare say she does. I expect that\xe2\x80\x99s it.--What an idiot I am not to\r\nhave thought of that before.\xe2\x80\x99 He sighed,--and refilled his glass. \xe2\x80\x98He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na lucky chap, whoever he is. I\xe2\x80\x99d--I\xe2\x80\x99d like to tell him so.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99d like to tell him so?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He\xe2\x80\x99s such a jolly lucky chap, you know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Possibly,--but his jolly good luck is your jolly bad luck. Would you\r\nbe willing to resign her to him without a word?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If she loves him.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But you say you love her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course I do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose that, because I love her, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to see\r\nher happy?--I\xe2\x80\x99m not such a beast!--I\xe2\x80\x99d sooner see her happy than\r\nanything else in all the world.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I see.--Even happy with another?--I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid that my philosophy is not\r\nlike yours. If I loved Miss Lindon, and she loved, say, Jones, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nafraid I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t feel like that towards Jones at all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What would you feel like?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Murder.--Percy, you come home with me,--we\xe2\x80\x99ve begun the night\r\ntogether, let\xe2\x80\x99s end it together,--and I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you one of the finest\r\nnotions for committing murder on a scale of real magnificence you ever\r\ndreamed of. I should like to make use of it to show my feelings towards\r\nthe supposititious Jones,--he\xe2\x80\x99d know what I felt for him when once he\r\nhad been introduced to it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nPercy went with me without a word. He had not had much to drink, but it\r\nhad been too much for him, and he was in a condition of maundering\r\nsentimentality. I got him into a cab. We dashed along Piccadilly.\r\n\r\nHe was silent, and sat looking in front of him with an air of vacuous\r\nsullenness which ill-became his cast of countenance. I bade the cabman\r\npass though Lowndes Square. As we passed the Apostle\xe2\x80\x99s I pulled him up.\r\nI pointed out the place to Woodville.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You see, Percy, that\xe2\x80\x99s Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s house!--that\xe2\x80\x99s the house of the\r\nman who went away with Marjorie!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99 Words came from him slowly, with a quite unnecessary stress on\r\neach. \xe2\x80\x98Because he made a speech.--I\xe2\x80\x99d like to make a speech.--One day\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll make a speech.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Because he made a speech,--only that, and nothing more! When a man\r\nspeaks with an Apostle\xe2\x80\x99s tongue, he can witch any woman in the\r\nland.--Hallo, who\xe2\x80\x99s that?--Lessingham, is that you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI saw, or thought I saw, someone, or something, glide up the steps, and\r\nwithdraw into the shadow of the doorway, as if unwilling to be seen.\r\nWhen I hailed no one answered. I called again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t be shy, my friend!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI sprang out of the cab, ran across the pavement, and up the steps. To\r\nmy surprise, there was no one in the doorway. It seemed incredible, but\r\nthe place was empty. I felt about me with my hands, as if I had been\r\nplaying at blind man\xe2\x80\x99s buff, and grasped at vacancy. I came down a step\r\nor two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ostensibly, there\xe2\x80\x99s a vacuum,--which nature abhors.--I say, driver,\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t you see someone come up the steps?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought I did, sir,--I could have sworn I did.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So could I.--It\xe2\x80\x99s very odd.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perhaps whoever it was has gone into the \xe2\x80\x99ouse, sir.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how. We should have heard the door open, if we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen\r\nit,--and we should have seen it, it\xe2\x80\x99s not so dark as that.--I\xe2\x80\x99ve half a\r\nmind to ring the bell and inquire.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do that if I was you, sir,--you jump in, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll get\r\nalong. This is Mr Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s,--the great Mr Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI believe the cabman thought that I was drunk,--and not respectable\r\nenough to claim acquaintance with the great Mr Lessingham.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Wake up, Woodville! Do you know I believe there\xe2\x80\x99s some mystery about\r\nthis place,--I feel assured of it. I feel as if I were in the presence\r\nof something uncanny,--something which I can neither see, nor touch,\r\nnor hear.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe cabman bent down from his seat, wheedling me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Jump in, sir, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll be getting along.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI jumped in, and we got along,--but not far. Before we had gone a dozen\r\nyards, I was out again, without troubling the driver to stop. He pulled\r\nup, aggrieved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, sir, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter now? You\xe2\x80\x99ll be damaging yourself before\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve done, and then you\xe2\x80\x99ll be blaming me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI had caught sight of a cat crouching in the shadow of the railings,--a\r\nblack one. That cat was my quarry. Either the creature was unusually\r\nsleepy, or slow, or stupid, or it had lost its wits--which a cat seldom\r\ndoes lose!--anyhow, without making an attempt to escape it allowed me\r\nto grab it by the nape of the neck.\r\n\r\nSo soon as we were inside my laboratory, I put the cat into my glass\r\nbox. Percy stared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What have you put it there for?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That, my dear Percy, is what you are shortly about to see. You are\r\nabout to be the witness of an experiment which, to a legislator--such\r\nas you are!--ought to be of the greatest possible interest. I am going\r\nto demonstrate, on a small scale, the action of the force which, on a\r\nlarge scale, I propose to employ on behalf of my native land.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe showed no signs of being interested. Sinking into a chair, he\r\nrecommenced his wearisome reiteration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hate cats!--Do let it go!--I\xe2\x80\x99m always miserable when there\xe2\x80\x99s a cat\r\nin the room.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nonsense,--that\xe2\x80\x99s your fancy! What you want\xe2\x80\x99s a taste of\r\nwhisky--you\xe2\x80\x99ll be as chirpy as a cricket.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t want anything more to drink!--I\xe2\x80\x99ve had too much already!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI paid no heed to what he said. I poured two stiff doses into a couple\r\nof tumblers. Without seeming to be aware of what it was that he was\r\ndoing he disposed of the better half of the one I gave him at a\r\ndraught. Putting his glass upon the table, he dropped his head upon his\r\nhands, and groaned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What would Marjorie think of me if she saw me now?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Think?--nothing. Why should she think of a man like you, when she has\r\nso much better fish to fry?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m feeling frightfully ill!--I\xe2\x80\x99ll be drunk before I\xe2\x80\x99ve done!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then be drunk!--only, for gracious sake, be lively drunk, not deadly\r\ndoleful.--Cheer up, Percy!\xe2\x80\x99 I clapped him on the shoulder,--almost\r\nknocking him off his seat on to the floor. \xe2\x80\x98I am now going to show you\r\nthat little experiment of which I was speaking!--You see that cat?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course I see it!--the beast!--I wish you\xe2\x80\x99d let it go!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why should I let it go?--Do you know whose cat that is? That cat\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nPaul Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Paul Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, Paul Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s,--the man who made the speech,--the man whom\r\nMarjorie went away with.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How do you know it\xe2\x80\x99s his?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know it is, but I believe it is,--I choose to believe it\r\nis!--I intend to believe it is!--It was outside his house, therefore\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s his cat,--that\xe2\x80\x99s how I argue. I can\xe2\x80\x99t get Lessingham inside that\r\nbox, so I get his cat instead.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whatever for?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You shall see.--You observe how happy it is?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It don\xe2\x80\x99t seem happy.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99ve all our ways of seeming happy,--that\xe2\x80\x99s its way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe creature was behaving like a cat gone mad, dashing itself against\r\nthe sides of its glass prison, leaping to and fro, and from side to\r\nside, squealing with rage, or with terror, or with both. Perhaps it\r\nforesaw what was coming,--there is no fathoming the intelligence of\r\nwhat we call the lower animals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s a funny way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We some of us have funny ways, beside cats. Now, attention! Observe\r\nthis little toy,--you\xe2\x80\x99ve seen something of its kind before. It\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nspring gun; you pull the spring--drop the charge into the\r\nbarrel--release the spring--and the charge is fired. I\xe2\x80\x99ll unlock this\r\nsafe, which is built into the wall. It\xe2\x80\x99s a letter lock, the combination\r\njust now, is \xe2\x80\x9cwhisky,\xe2\x80\x9d--you see, that\xe2\x80\x99s a hint to you. You\xe2\x80\x99ll notice\r\nthe safe is strongly made,--it\xe2\x80\x99s air-tight, fire-proof, the outer\r\ncasing is of triple-plated drill-proof steel,--the contents are\r\nvaluable--to me!--and devilish dangerous,--I\xe2\x80\x99d pity the thief who, in\r\nhis innocent ignorance, broke in to steal. Look inside,--you see it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfull of balls,--glass balls, each in its own little separate nest;\r\nlight as feathers; transparent,--you can see right through them. Here\r\nare a couple, like tiny pills.
