In\r\nmatters of prestidigitation, Champnell, we Westerns are among the\r\nrudiments,--we\xe2\x80\x99ve everything to learn,--Orientals leave us at the post.\r\nIf their civilisation\xe2\x80\x99s what we\xe2\x80\x99re pleased to call extinct, their\r\nconjuring--when you get to know it!--is all alive oh!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe moved towards the door. As he went he slipped, or seemed to, all but\r\nstumbling on to his knees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Something tripped me up,--what\xe2\x80\x99s this?\xe2\x80\x99 He was stamping on the floor\r\nwith his foot. \xe2\x80\x98Here\xe2\x80\x99s a board loose. Come and lend me a hand, one of\r\nyou fellows, to get it up. Who knows what mystery\xe2\x80\x99s beneath?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI went to his aid. As he said, a board in the floor was loose. His\r\nstepping on it unawares had caused his stumble. Together we prised it\r\nout of its place,--Lessingham standing by and watching us the while.\r\nHaving removed it, we peered into the cavity it disclosed.\r\n\r\nThere was something there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why,\xe2\x80\x99 cried Atherton, \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s a woman\xe2\x80\x99s clothing!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n [IMAGE: images/img_279.jpg\r\n CAPTION: THEY STARED AT ME IN SILENCE AS I DRAGGED THESE OUT AND LAID\r\n THEM ON THE FLOOR.]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XXXVIII.\r\n THE REST OF THE FIND\r\n\r\nIt was a woman\xe2\x80\x99s clothing, beyond a doubt, all thrown in anyhow,--as if\r\nthe person who had placed it there had been in a desperate hurry. An\r\nentire outfit was there, shoes, stockings, body linen, corsets, and\r\nall,--even to hat, gloves, and hairpins;--these latter were mixed up\r\nwith the rest of the garments in strange confusion. It seemed plain\r\nthat whoever had worn those clothes had been stripped to the skin.\r\n\r\nLessingham and Sydney stared at me in silence as I dragged them out and\r\nlaid them on the floor. The dress was at the bottom,--it was an alpaca,\r\nof a pretty shade in blue, bedecked with lace and ribbons, as is the\r\nfashion of the hour, and lined with sea-green silk. It had perhaps been\r\na \xe2\x80\x98charming confection\xe2\x80\x99 once--and that a very recent one!--but now it\r\nwas all soiled and creased and torn and tumbled. The two spectators\r\nmade a simultaneous pounce at it as I brought it to the light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My God!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Sydney, \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s Marjorie\xe2\x80\x99s!--she was wearing it when I\r\nsaw her last!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s Marjorie\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x99 gasped Lessingham,--he was clutching at the ruined\r\ncostume, staring at it like a man who has just received sentence of\r\ndeath. \xe2\x80\x98She wore it when she was with me yesterday,--I told her how it\r\nsuited her, and how pretty it was!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was silence,--it was an eloquent find; it spoke for itself. The\r\ntwo men gazed at the heap of feminine glories,--it might have been the\r\nmost wonderful sight they ever had seen. Lessingham was the first to\r\nspeak,--his face had all at once grown grey and haggard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What has happened to her?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI replied to his question with another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Are you sure this is Miss Lindon\xe2\x80\x99s dress?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am sure,--and were proof needed, here it is.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe had found the pocket, and was turning out the contents. There was a\r\npurse, which contained money and some visiting cards on which were her\r\nname and address; a small bunch of keys, with her nameplate attached; a\r\nhandkerchief, with her initials in a corner. The question of ownership\r\nwas placed beyond a doubt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You see,\xe2\x80\x99 said Lessingham, exhibiting the money which was in the\r\npurse, \xe2\x80\x98it is not robbery which has been attempted. Here are two\r\nten-pound notes, and one for five, besides gold and silver,--over\r\nthirty pounds in all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAtherton, who had been turning over the accumulation of rubbish between\r\nthe joists, proclaimed another find.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here are her rings, and watch, and a bracelet,--no, it certainly does\r\nnot look as if theft had been an object.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLessingham was glowering at him with knitted brows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have to thank you for this.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSydney was unwontedly meek.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are hard on me, Lessingham, harder than I deserve,--I had rather\r\nhave thrown away my own life than have suffered misadventure to have\r\ncome to her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yours are idle words. Had you not meddled this would not have\r\nhappened. A fool works more mischief with his folly than of malice\r\nprepense. If hurt has befallen Marjorie Lindon you shall account for it\r\nto me with your life\xe2\x80\x99s blood.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Let it be so,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sydney. \xe2\x80\x98I am content. If hurt has come to\r\nMarjorie, God knows that I am willing enough that death should come to\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhile they wrangled, I continued to search. A little to one side, under\r\nthe flooring which was still intact, I saw something gleam. By\r\nstretching out my hand, I could just manage to reach it,--it was a long\r\nplait of woman\xe2\x80\x99s hair. It had been cut off at the roots,--so close to\r\nthe head in one place that the scalp itself had been cut, so that the\r\nhair was clotted with blood.\r\n\r\nThey were so occupied with each other that they took no notice of me. I\r\nhad to call their attention to my discovery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Gentlemen, I fear that I have here something which will distress\r\nyou,--is not this Miss Lindon\xe2\x80\x99s hair?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey recognised it on the instant. Lessingham, snatching it from my\r\nhands, pressed it to his lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This is mine,--I shall at least have something.\xe2\x80\x99 He spoke with a\r\ngrimness which was a little startling. He held the silken tresses at\r\narm\xe2\x80\x99s length. \xe2\x80\x98This points to murder,--foul, cruel, causeless murder.\r\nAs I live, I will devote my all,--money, time, reputation!--to gaining\r\nvengeance on the wretch who did this deed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAtherton chimed in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To that I say, Amen!\xe2\x80\x99 He lifted his hand. \xe2\x80\x98God is my witness!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It seems to me, gentlemen, that we move too fast,--to my mind it does\r\nnot by any means of necessity point to murder. On the contrary, I doubt\r\nif murder has been done. Indeed, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind owning that I have a\r\ntheory of my own which points all the other way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLessingham caught me by the sleeve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Champnell, tell me your theory.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will, a little later. Of course it may be altogether wrong;--though\r\nI fancy it is not; I will explain my reasons when we come to talk of\r\nit. But, at present, there are things which must be done.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I vote for tearing up every board in the house!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Sydney. \xe2\x80\x98And\r\nfor pulling the whole infernal place to pieces. It\xe2\x80\x99s a conjurer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nden.--I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised if cabby\xe2\x80\x99s old gent is staring at us all\r\nthe while from some peephole of his own.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWe examined the entire house, methodically, so far as we were able,\r\ninch by inch. Not another board proved loose,--to lift those which were\r\nnailed down required tools, and those we were without. We sounded all\r\nthe walls,--with the exception of the party walls they were the usual\r\nlath and plaster constructions, and showed no signs of having been\r\ntampered with. The ceilings were intact; if anything was concealed in\r\nthem it must have been there some time,--the cement was old and dirty.\r\nWe took the closet to pieces; examined the chimneys; peered into the\r\nkitchen oven and the copper;--in short, we pried into everything which,\r\nwith the limited means at our disposal, could be pried into,--without\r\nresult. At the end we found ourselves dusty, dirty, and discomfited.\r\nThe cabman\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98old gent\xe2\x80\x99 remained as much a mystery as ever, and no\r\nfurther trace had been discovered of Miss Lindon.\r\n\r\nAtherton made no effort to disguise his chagrin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now what\xe2\x80\x99s to be done? There seems to be just nothing in the place at\r\nall, and yet that there is, and that it\xe2\x80\x99s the key to the whole\r\nconfounded business I should be disposed to swear.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In that case I would suggest that you should stay and look for it. The\r\ncabman can go and look for the requisite tools, or a workman to assist\r\nyou, if you like. For my part it appears to me that evidence of another\r\nsort is, for the moment, of paramount importance; and I propose to\r\ncommence my search for it by making a call at the house which is over\r\nthe way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI had observed, on our arrival, that the road only contained two houses\r\nwhich were in anything like a finished state,--that which we were in,\r\nand another, some fifty or sixty yards further down, on the opposite\r\nside. It was to this I referred. The twain immediately proffered their\r\ncompanionship.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will come with you,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr Lessingham.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And I,\xe2\x80\x99 echoed Sydney. \xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99ll leave this sweet homestead in charge of\r\nthe cabman,--I\xe2\x80\x99ll pull it to pieces afterwards.\xe2\x80\x99 He went out and spoke\r\nto the driver. \xe2\x80\x98Cabby, we\xe2\x80\x99re going to pay a visit to the little crib\r\nover there,--you keep an eye on this one. And if you see a sign of\r\nanyone being about the place,--living, or dead, or anyhow--you give me\r\na yell. I shall be on the lookout, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be with you before you can\r\nsay Jack Robinson.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You bet I\xe2\x80\x99ll yell,--I\xe2\x80\x99ll raise the hair right off you.\xe2\x80\x99 The fellow\r\ngrinned. \xe2\x80\x98But I don\xe2\x80\x99t know if you gents are hiring me by the day,--I\r\nwant to change my horse; he ought to have been in his stable a couple\r\nof hours ago.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Never mind your horse,--let him rest a couple of hours extra to-morrow\r\nto make up for those he has lost to-day. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take care you don\xe2\x80\x99t lose\r\nanything by this little job,--or your horse either.--By the way, look\r\nhere,--this will be better than yelling.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTaking a revolver out of his trousers\xe2\x80\x99 pocket he handed it up to the\r\ngrinning driver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If that old gent of yours does appear, you have a pop at him,--I shall\r\nhear that easier than a yell. You can put a bullet through him if you\r\nlike,--I give you my word it won\xe2\x80\x99t be murder.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t care if it is,\xe2\x80\x99 declared the cabman, handling the weapon like\r\none who was familiar with arms of precision. \xe2\x80\x98I used to fancy my\r\nrevolver shooting when I was with the colours, and if I do get a chance\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll put a shot through the old hunks, if only to prove to you that I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nno liar.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhether the man was in earnest or not I could not tell,--nor whether\r\nAtherton meant what he said in answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you shoot him I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you fifty pounds.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98All right!\xe2\x80\x99 The driver laughed. \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll do my best to earn that fifty!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XXXIX.\r\n MISS LOUISA COLEMAN\r\n\r\nThat the house over the way was tenanted was plain to all the\r\nworld,--at least one occupant sat gazing through the window of the\r\nfirst floor front room. An old woman in a cap,--one of those large\r\nold-fashioned caps which our grandmothers used to wear, tied with\r\nstrings under the chin. It was a bow window, and as she was seated in\r\nthe bay looking right in our direction she could hardly have failed to\r\nsee us as we advanced,--indeed she continued to stare at us all the\r\nwhile with placid calmness. Yet I knocked once, twice, and yet again\r\nwithout the slightest notice being taken of my summons.\r\n\r\nSydney gave expression to his impatience in his own peculiar vein.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Knockers in this part of the world seem intended for ornament\r\nonly,--nobody seems to pay any attention to them when they\xe2\x80\x99re used. The\r\nold lady upstairs must be either deaf or dotty.\xe2\x80\x99 He went out into the\r\nroad to see if she still was there. \xe2\x80\x98She\xe2\x80\x99s looking at me as calmly as\r\nyou please,--what does she think we\xe2\x80\x99re doing here, I wonder; playing a\r\ntune on her front door by way of a little amusement?--Madam!\xe2\x80\x99 He took\r\noff his hat and waved it to her. \xe2\x80\x98Madam! might I observe that if you\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t condescend to notice that we\xe2\x80\x99re here your front door will run the\r\nrisk of being severely injured!--She don\xe2\x80\x99t care for me any more than if\r\nI was nothing at all,--sound another tattoo upon that knocker. Perhaps\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s so deaf that nothing short of a cataclysmal uproar will reach her\r\nauditory nerves.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe immediately proved, however, that she was nothing of the sort.\r\nHardly had the sounds of my further knocking died away than, throwing\r\nup the window, she thrust out her head and addressed me in a fashion\r\nwhich, under the circumstances, was as unexpected as it was uncalled\r\nfor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, young man, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t be in such a hurry!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSydney explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pardon me, madam, it\xe2\x80\x99s not so much a hurry we\xe2\x80\x99re in as pressed for\r\ntime,--this is a matter of life and death.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe turned her attention to Sydney,--speaking with a frankness for\r\nwhich, I imagine, he was unprepared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t want none of your imperence, young man. I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen you\r\nbefore,--you\xe2\x80\x99ve been hanging about here the whole day long!--and I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like the looks of you, and so I\xe2\x80\x99ll let you know. That\xe2\x80\x99s my front\r\ndoor, and that\xe2\x80\x99s my knocker,--I\xe2\x80\x99ll come down and open when I like, but\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m not going to be hurried, and if the knocker\xe2\x80\x99s so much as touched\r\nagain, I won\xe2\x80\x99t come down at all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe closed the window with a bang. Sydney seemed divided between mirth\r\nand indignation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s a nice old lady, on my honour,--one of the good old crusty\r\nsort. Agreeable characters this neighbourhood seems to grow,--a sojourn\r\nhereabouts should do one good. Unfortunately I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel disposed just\r\nnow to stand and kick my heels in the road.\xe2\x80\x99 Again saluting the old\r\ndame by raising his hat he shouted to her at the top of his voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Madam, I beg ten thousand pardons for troubling you, but this is a\r\nmatter in which every second is of vital importance,--would you allow\r\nme to ask you one or two questions?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nUp went the window; out came the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, young man, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t put yourself out to holler at me,--I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe hollered at! I\xe2\x80\x99ll come down and open that door in five minutes by\r\nthe clock on my mantelpiece, and not a moment before.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe fiat delivered, down came the window. Sydney looked rueful,--he\r\nconsulted his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you think, Champnell, but I really doubt if this\r\ncomfortable creature can tell us anything worth waiting another five\r\nminutes to hear. We mustn\xe2\x80\x99t let the grass grow under our feet, and time\r\nis getting on.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI was of a different opinion,--and said so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, Atherton, that I can\xe2\x80\x99t agree with you. She seems to have\r\nnoticed you hanging about all day; and it is at least possible that she\r\nhas noticed a good deal which would be well worth our hearing. What\r\nmore promising witness are we likely to find?--her house is the only\r\none which overlooks the one we have just quitted. I am of opinion that\r\nit may not only prove well worth our while to wait five minutes, but\r\nalso that it would be as well, if possible, not to offend her by the\r\nway. She\xe2\x80\x99s not likely to afford us the information we require if you\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good. If that\xe2\x80\x99s what you think I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I\xe2\x80\x99m willing to wait,--only\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s to be hoped that that clock upon her mantelpiece moves quicker\r\nthan its mistress.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nPresently, when about a minute had gone, he called to the cabman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Seen a sign of anything?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe cabman shouted back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ne\xe2\x80\x99er a sign,--you\xe2\x80\x99ll hear a sound of popguns when I do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThose five minutes did seem long ones. But at last Sydney, from his\r\npost of vantage in the road, informed us that the old lady was moving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She\xe2\x80\x99s getting up;--she\xe2\x80\x99s leaving the window;--let\xe2\x80\x99s hope to goodness\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s coming down to open the door. That\xe2\x80\x99s been the longest five\r\nminutes I\xe2\x80\x99ve known.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI could hear uncertain footsteps descending the stairs. They came along\r\nthe passage. The door was opened--\xe2\x80\x98on the chain.\xe2\x80\x99 The old lady peered\r\nat us through an aperture of about six inches.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you young men think you\xe2\x80\x99re after, but have all three\r\nof you in my house I won\xe2\x80\x99t. I\xe2\x80\x99ll have him and you\xe2\x80\x99--a skinny finger was\r\npointed to Lessingham and me; then it was directed towards\r\nAtherton--\xe2\x80\x98but have him I won\xe2\x80\x99t. So if it\xe2\x80\x99s anything particular you\r\nwant to say to me, you\xe2\x80\x99ll just tell him to go away.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nOn hearing this Sydney\xe2\x80\x99s humility was abject. His hat was in his\r\nhand,--he bent himself double.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Suffer me to make you a million apologies, madam, if I have in any way\r\noffended you; nothing, I assure you, could have been farther from my\r\nintention, or from my thoughts.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t want none of your apologies, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want none of you\r\nneither; I don\xe2\x80\x99t like the looks of you, and so I tell you. Before I let\r\nanybody into my house you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to sling your hook.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe door was banged in our faces. I turned to Sydney.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The sooner you go the better it will be for us. You can wait for us\r\nover the way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe shrugged his shoulders, and groaned,--half in jest, half in earnest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If I must I suppose I must,--it\xe2\x80\x99s the first time I\xe2\x80\x99ve been refused\r\nadmittance to a lady\xe2\x80\x99s house in all my life! What have I done to\r\ndeserve this thing?--If you keep me waiting long I\xe2\x80\x99ll tear that\r\ninfernal den to pieces!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe sauntered across the road, viciously kicking the stones as he went.\r\nThe door reopened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Has that other young man gone?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then now I\xe2\x80\x99ll let you in. Have him inside my house I won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe chain was removed. Lessingham and I entered. Then the door was\r\nrefastened and the chain replaced. Our hostess showed us into the front\r\nroom on the ground floor; it was sparsely furnished and not too\r\nclean,--but there were chairs enough for us to sit upon; which she\r\ninsisted on our occupying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sit down, do,--I can\xe2\x80\x99t abide to see folks standing; it gives me the\r\nfidgets.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSo soon as we were seated, without any overture on our parts she\r\nplunged _in medias res_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know what it is you\xe2\x80\x99ve come about,--I know! You want me to tell you\r\nwho it is as lives in the house over the road. Well, I can tell\r\nyou,--and I dare bet a shilling that I\xe2\x80\x99m about the only one who can.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI inclined my head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed. Is that so, madam?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe was huffed at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t madam me,--I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear none of your lip service. I\xe2\x80\x99m a\r\nplain-spoken woman, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I am, and I like other people\xe2\x80\x99s tongues\r\nto be as plain as mine. My name\xe2\x80\x99s Miss Louisa Coleman; but I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ngenerally called Miss Coleman,--I\xe2\x80\x99m only called Louisa by my relatives.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSince she was apparently between seventy and eighty--and looked every\r\nyear of her apparent age--I deemed that possible. Miss Coleman was\r\nevidently a character. If one was desirous of getting information out\r\nof her it would be necessary to allow her to impart it in her own\r\nmanner,--to endeavour to induce her to impart it in anybody else\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwould be time clean wasted. We had Sydney\xe2\x80\x99s fate before our eyes.\r\n\r\nShe started with a sort of roundabout preamble.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This property is mine; it was left me by my uncle, the late George\r\nHenry Jobson,--he\xe2\x80\x99s buried in Hammersmith Cemetery just over the\r\nway,--he left me the whole of it. It\xe2\x80\x99s one of the finest building sites\r\nnear London, and it increases in value every year, and I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to\r\nlet it for another twenty, by which time the value will have more than\r\ntrebled,--so if that is what you\xe2\x80\x99ve come about, as heaps of people do,\r\nyou might have saved yourselves the trouble. I keep the boards\r\nstanding, just to let people know that the ground is to let,--though,\r\nas I say, it won\xe2\x80\x99t be for another twenty years, when it\xe2\x80\x99ll be for the\r\nerection of high-class mansions only, same as there is in Grosvenor\r\nSquare,--no shops or public houses, and none of your shanties. I live\r\nin this place just to keep an eye upon the property,--and as for the\r\nhouse over the way, I\xe2\x80\x99ve never tried to let it, and it never has been\r\nlet, not until a month ago, when, one morning, I had this letter. You\r\ncan see it if you like.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe handed me a greasy envelope which she ferreted out of a capacious\r\npocket which was suspended from her waist, and which she had to lift up\r\nher skirt to reach. The envelope was addressed, in unformed characters,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Miss Louisa Coleman, The Rhododendrons, Convolvulus Avenue, High Oaks\r\nPark, West Kensington.\xe2\x80\x99--I felt, if the writer had not been of a\r\nhumorous turn of mind, and drawn on his imagination, and this really\r\nwas the lady\xe2\x80\x99s correct address, then there must be something in a name.\r\n\r\nThe letter within was written in the same straggling, characterless\r\ncaligraphy,--I should have said, had I been asked offhand, that the\r\nwhole thing was the composition of a servant girl. The composition was\r\nabout on a par with the writing.\r\n\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x98The undersigned would be oblidged if Miss Coleman would let her empty\r\n house. I do not know the rent but send fifty pounds. If more will send.\r\n Please address, Mohamed el Kheir, Post Office, Sligo Street, London.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\nIt struck me as being as singular an application for a tenancy as I\r\nremembered to have encountered. When I passed it on to Lessingham, he\r\nseemed to think so too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This is a curious letter, Miss Coleman.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So I thought,--and still more so when I found the fifty pounds inside.\r\nThere were five ten-pound notes, all loose, and the letter not even\r\nregistered. If I had been asked what was the rent of the house, I\r\nshould have said, at the most, not more than twenty pounds,--because,\r\nbetween you and me, it wants a good bit of doing up, and is hardly fit\r\nto live in as it stands.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI had had sufficient evidence of the truth of this altogether apart\r\nfrom the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s frank admission.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, for all he could have done to help himself I might have kept the\r\nmoney, and only sent him a receipt for a quarter. And some folks would\r\nhave done,--but I\xe2\x80\x99m not one of that sort myself, and shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care to\r\nbe. So I sent this here party,--I never could pronounce his name, and\r\nnever shall--a receipt for a year.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMiss Coleman paused to smooth her apron, and consider.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, the receipt should have reached this here party on the Thursday\r\nmorning, as it were,--I posted it on the Wednesday night, and on the\r\nThursday, after breakfast, I thought I\xe2\x80\x99d go over the way to see if\r\nthere was any little thing I could do,--because there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly a\r\nwhole pane of glass in the place,--when I all but went all of a heap.\r\nWhen I looked across the road, blessed if the party wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in\r\nalready,--at least as much as he ever was in, which, so far as I can\r\nmake out, never has been anything particular,--though how he had got\r\nin, unless it was through a window in the middle of the night, is more\r\nthan I should care to say,--there was nobody in the house when I went\r\nto bed, that I could pretty nearly take my Bible oath,--yet there was\r\nthe blind up at the parlour, and, what\xe2\x80\x99s more, it was down, and it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeen down pretty nearly ever since.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I says to myself, \xe2\x80\x9cfor right down imperence this beats\r\nanything,--why he\xe2\x80\x99s in the place before he knows if I\xe2\x80\x99ll let him have\r\nit. Perhaps he thinks I haven\xe2\x80\x99t got a word to say in the matter,--fifty\r\npounds or no fifty pounds, I\xe2\x80\x99ll soon show him.\xe2\x80\x9d So I slips on my\r\nbonnet, and I walks over the road, and I hammers at the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, I have seen people hammering since then, many a one, and how\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ve kept it up has puzzled me,--for an hour, some of them,--but I\r\nwas the first one as begun it. I hammers, and I hammers, and I kept on\r\nhammering, but it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t no more use than if I\xe2\x80\x99d been hammering at a\r\ntombstone. So I starts rapping at the window, but that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t no use\r\nneither. So I goes round behind, and I hammers at the back door,--but\r\nthere, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make anyone hear nohow. So I says to myself, \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps\r\nthe party as is in, ain\xe2\x80\x99t in, in a manner of speaking; but I\xe2\x80\x99ll keep an\r\neye on the house, and when he is in I\xe2\x80\x99ll take care that he ain\xe2\x80\x99t out\r\nagain before I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a word to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So I come back home, and as I said I would, I kept an eye on the house\r\nthe whole of that livelong day, but never a soul went either out or in.\r\nBut the next day, which it was a Friday, I got out of bed about five\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, to see if it was raining, through my having an idea of taking\r\na little excursion if the weather was fine, when I see a party coming\r\ndown the road. He had on one of them dirty-coloured bed-cover sort of\r\nthings, and it was wrapped all over his head and round his body, like,\r\nas I have been told, them there Arabs wear,--and, indeed, I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen\r\nthem in them myself at West Brompton, when they was in the exhibition\r\nthere. It was quite fine, and broad day, and I see him as plainly as I\r\nsee you,--he comes skimming along at a tear of a pace, pulls up at the\r\nhouse over the way, opens the front door, and lets himself in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cSo,\xe2\x80\x9d I says to myself, \xe2\x80\x9cthere you are. I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you that landladies have\r\ntheir rights, like other Christians, in this country, however it may be\r\nin yours.\xe2\x80\x9d So I kept an eye on the house, to see that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t go out\r\nagain, and nobody never didn\xe2\x80\x99t, and between seven and eight I goes and\r\nI knocks at the door,--because I thought to myself that the earlier I\r\nwas the better it might be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you\xe2\x80\x99ll believe me, no more notice was taken of me than if I was one\r\nof the dead. I hammers, and I hammers, till my wrist was aching, I\r\ndaresay I hammered twenty times,--and then I went round to the back\r\ndoor, and I hammers at that,--but it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the least good in the\r\nworld. I was that provoked to think I should be treated as if I was\r\nnothing and nobody, by a dirty foreigner, who went about in a bed-gown\r\nthrough the public streets, that it was all I could do to hold myself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I comes round to the front again, and I starts hammering at the\r\nwindow, with every knuckle on my hands, and I calls out, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m Miss\r\nLouisa Coleman, and I\xe2\x80\x99m the owner of this house, and you can\xe2\x80\x99t deceive\r\nme,--I saw you come in, and you\xe2\x80\x99re in now, and if you don\xe2\x80\x99t come and\r\nspeak to me this moment I\xe2\x80\x99ll have the police.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98All of a sudden, when I was least expecting it, and was hammering my\r\nvery hardest at the pane, up goes the blind, and up goes the window\r\ntoo, and the most awful-looking creature ever I heard of, not to\r\nmention seeing, puts his head right into my face,--he was more like a\r\nhideous baboon than anything else, let alone a man. I was struck all of\r\na heap, and plumps down on the little wall, and all but tumbles head\r\nover heels backwards. And he starts shrieking, in a sort of a kind of\r\nEnglish, and in such a voice as I\xe2\x80\x99d never heard the like,--it was like\r\na rusty steam engine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cGo away! go away! I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you! I will not have you,--never! You\r\nhave your fifty pounds,--you have your money,--that is the whole of\r\nyou,--that is all you want! You come to me no more!--never!--never no\r\nmore!--or you be sorry!--Go away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I did go away, and that as fast as ever my legs would carry me,--what\r\nwith his looks, and what with his voice, and what with the way that he\r\nwent on, I was nothing but a mass of trembling. As for answering him\r\nback, or giving him a piece of my mind, as I had meant to, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave done it not for a thousand pounds. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind confessing,\r\nbetween you and me, that I had to swallow four cups of tea, right\r\nstraight away, before my nerves was steady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I says to myself, when I did feel, as it might be, a little\r\nmore easy, \xe2\x80\x9cyou never have let that house before, and now you\xe2\x80\x99ve let it\r\nwith a vengeance,--so you have. If that there new tenant of yours isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthe greatest villain that ever went unhung it must be because he\xe2\x80\x99s got\r\nnear relations what\xe2\x80\x99s as bad as himself,--because two families like his\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m sure there can\xe2\x80\x99t be. But from that hour to this I\xe2\x80\x99ve never spoken to the man,\r\nand never wanted to, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, not of my own free will, not for a\r\nshilling a time,--that face of his will haunt me if I live till Noah,\r\nas the saying is. I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen\r\nthem hammer and hammer at that front door, but never a one have I seen\r\nlet in,--or yet seen taken any notice of, and I think I may say, and\r\nyet tell no lie, that I\xe2\x80\x99ve scarcely took my eye off the house since\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s been inside it, over and over again in the middle of the night\r\nhave I got up to have a look, so that I\xe2\x80\x99ve not missed much that has\r\ntook place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s puzzled me is the noises that\xe2\x80\x99s come from the house. I have\r\nthought, and more than once, that the devil himself must be in that\r\nfront room, let alone all the rest of his demons. I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen them inside the house, at the\r\nwindows, upstairs and downstairs, as it seemed to me, a dozen at a time.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XL.\r\n WHAT MISS COLEMAN SAW THROUGH THE WINDOW\r\n\r\nAs Miss Coleman had paused, as if her narrative was approaching a\r\nconclusion, I judged it expedient to make an attempt to bring the\r\nrecord as quickly as possible up to date.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I take it, Miss Coleman, that you have observed what has occurred in\r\nthe house to-day.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe tightened her nut-cracker jaws and glared at me disdainfully,--her\r\ndignity was ruffled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m coming to it, aren\xe2\x80\x99t I?--if you\xe2\x80\x99ll let me. That Arab party has been flitting about\r\nlike a creature possessed,--I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him going in and out twenty times\r\na day. This morning--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe paused,--to fix her eyes on Lessingham. As I was taking the\r\nmilk, the man said to me, \xe2\x80\x9cHollo, Miss Coleman, here\xe2\x80\x99s your friend\r\ncoming along.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cWhat friend?\xe2\x80\x9d I says,--for I ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no friends, as I\r\nknow, round here, nor yet, I hope no enemies neither.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And I looks round, and there was the Arab party coming tearing down\r\nthe road, his bedcover thing all flying in the wind, and his arms\r\nstraight out in front of him,--I never did see anyone go at such a\r\npace. \xe2\x80\x9cMy goodness,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder he don\xe2\x80\x99t do himself an injury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder someone else don\xe2\x80\x99t do him an injury,\xe2\x80\x9d says the milkman. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nvery sight of him is enough to make my milk go sour.\xe2\x80\x9d And he picked up\r\nhis pail and went away quite grumpy,--though what that Arab party\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndone to him is more than I can say.--I have always noticed that\r\nmilkman\xe2\x80\x99s temper\xe2\x80\x99s short like his measure.
