God help us all!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I repeat that I doubt it. If you will be advised by me you will wait\r\nawhile before you arrive at any such conclusion.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAll at once there was a tapping at the window pane. Atherton was\r\nstaring at us from without.\r\n\r\nHe shouted through the glass,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come out of that, you fossils!--I\xe2\x80\x99ve news for you!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XLI.\r\n THE CONSTABLE,--HIS CLUE,--AND THE CAB\r\n\r\nMiss Coleman, getting up in a fluster, went hurrying to the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I won\xe2\x80\x99t have that young man in my house. I won\xe2\x80\x99t have him! Don\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nhim dare to put his nose across my doorstep.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI endeavoured to appease her perturbation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I promise you that he shall not come in, Miss Coleman. My friend here,\r\nand I, will go and speak to him outside.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe held the front door open just wide enough to enable Lessingham and\r\nme to slip through, then she shut it after us with a bang. She\r\nevidently had a strong objection to any intrusion on Sydney\xe2\x80\x99s part.\r\n\r\nStanding just without the gate he saluted us with a characteristic\r\nvigour which was scarcely flattering to our late hostess. Behind him\r\nwas a constable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope you two have been mewed in with that old pussy long enough.\r\nWhile you\xe2\x80\x99ve been tittle-tattling I\xe2\x80\x99ve been doing,--listen to what this\r\nbobby\xe2\x80\x99s got to say.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe constable, his thumbs thrust inside his belt, wore an indulgent\r\nsmile upon his countenance. He seemed to find Sydney amusing. He spoke\r\nin a deep bass voice,--as if it issued from his boots.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I\xe2\x80\x99ve got anything to say.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was plain that Sydney thought otherwise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You wait till I\xe2\x80\x99ve given this pretty pair of gossips a lead, officer,\r\nthen I\xe2\x80\x99ll trot you out.\xe2\x80\x99 He turned to us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98After I\xe2\x80\x99d poked my nose into every dashed hole in that infernal den,\r\nand been rewarded with nothing but a pain in the back for my trouble, I\r\nstood cooling my heels on the doorstep, wondering if I should fight the\r\ncabman, or get him to fight me, just to pass the time away,--for he\r\nsays he can box, and he looks it,--when who should come strolling along\r\nbut this magnificent example of the metropolitan constabulary.\xe2\x80\x99 He\r\nwaved his hand towards the policeman, whose grin grew wider. \xe2\x80\x98I looked\r\nat him, and he looked at me, and then when we\xe2\x80\x99d had enough of admiring\r\neach other\xe2\x80\x99s fine features and striking proportions, he said to me,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he gone?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cWho?--Baxter?--or Bob Brown?\xe2\x80\x9d He said, \xe2\x80\x9cNo, the\r\nArab.\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you know about any Arab?\xe2\x80\x9d He said, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I saw\r\nhim in the Broadway about three-quarters of an hour ago, and then,\r\nseeing you here, and the house all open, I wondered if he had gone for\r\ngood.\xe2\x80\x9d With that I almost jumped out of my skin, though you can bet\r\nyour life I never showed it. I said, \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know it was he?\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cIt was him right enough, there\xe2\x80\x99s no doubt about that. If you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nseen him once, you\xe2\x80\x99re not likely to forget him.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cWhere was he going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was talking to a cabman,--four-wheeler. He\xe2\x80\x99d got a great bundle on\r\nhis head,--wanted to take it inside with him. Cabman didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to see\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d That was enough for me,--I picked this most deserving officer up\r\nin my arms, and carried him across the road to you two fellows like a\r\nflash of lightning.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSince the policeman was six feet three or four, and more than\r\nsufficiently broad in proportion, his scarcely seemed the kind of\r\nfigure to be picked up in anybody\xe2\x80\x99s arms and carried like a \xe2\x80\x98flash of\r\nlightning,\xe2\x80\x99 which,--as his smile grew more indulgent, he himself\r\nappeared to think.\r\n\r\nStill, even allowing for Atherton\xe2\x80\x99s exaggeration, the news which he had\r\nbrought was sufficiently important. I questioned the constable upon my\r\nown account.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There is my card, officer, probably, before the day is over, a charge\r\nof a very serious character will be preferred against the person who\r\nhas been residing in the house over the way. In the meantime it is of\r\nthe utmost importance that a watch should be kept upon his movements. I\r\nsuppose you have no sort of doubt that the person you saw in the\r\nBroadway was the one in question?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not a morsel. I know him as well as I do my own brother,--we all do\r\nupon this beat. He\xe2\x80\x99s known amongst us as the Arab. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had my eye on\r\nhim ever since he came to the place. A queer fish he is. I always have\r\nsaid that he\xe2\x80\x99s up to some game or other. I never came across one like\r\nhim for flying about in all sorts of weather, at all hours of the\r\nnight, always tearing along as if for his life. As I was telling this\r\ngentleman I saw him in the Broadway,--well, now it\xe2\x80\x99s about an hour\r\nsince, perhaps a little more. I was coming on duty when I saw a crowd\r\nin front of the District Railway Station,--and there was the Arab,\r\nhaving a sort of argument with the cabman. He had a great bundle on his\r\nhead, five or six feet long, perhaps longer. He wanted to take this\r\ngreat bundle with him into the cab, and the cabman, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t see it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You didn\xe2\x80\x99t wait to see him drive off.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No,--I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t time. I was due at the station,--I was cutting it pretty\r\nfine as it was.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You didn\xe2\x80\x99t speak to him,--or to the cabman?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any business of mine you understand. The whole thing\r\njust caught my eye as I was passing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And you didn\xe2\x80\x99t take the cabman\xe2\x80\x99s number?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, well, as far as that goes it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t needful. I know the cabman,\r\nhis name and all about him, his stable\xe2\x80\x99s in Bradmore.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI whipped out my note-book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Give me his address.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what his Christian name is, Tom, I believe, but I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\nsure. Anyhow his surname\xe2\x80\x99s Ellis and his address is Church Mews, St\r\nJohn\xe2\x80\x99s Road, Bradmore,--I don\xe2\x80\x99t know his number, but any one will tell\r\nyou which is his place, if you ask for Four-Wheel Ellis,--that\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nname he\xe2\x80\x99s known by among his pals because of his driving a\r\nfour-wheeler.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you, officer. I am obliged to you.\xe2\x80\x99 Two half-crowns changed\r\nhands. \xe2\x80\x98If you will keep an eye on the house and advise me at the\r\naddress which you will find on my card, of any thing which takes place\r\nthere during the next few days, you will do me a service.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWe had clambered back into the hansom, the driver was just about to\r\nstart, when the constable was struck by a sudden thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98One moment, sir,--blessed if I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to forget the most\r\nimportant bit of all. I did hear him tell Ellis where to drive him\r\nto,--he kept saying it over and over again, in that queer lingo of his.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWaterloo Railway Station, Waterloo Railway Station.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nEllis, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll drive you to Waterloo Railway Station right enough, only\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m not going to have that bundle of yours inside my cab. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nroom for it, so you put it on the roof.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cTo Waterloo Railway Station,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Arab, \xe2\x80\x9cI take my bundle with me to Waterloo Railway\r\nStation,--I take it with me.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cWho says you don\xe2\x80\x99t take it with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Ellis. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can take it, and twenty more besides, for all I care,\r\nonly you don\xe2\x80\x99t take it inside my cab,--put it on the roof.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cI take it\r\nwith me to Waterloo Railway Station,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Arab, and there they\r\nwere, wrangling and jangling, and neither seeming to be able to make\r\nout what the other was after, and the people all laughing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Waterloo Railway Station,--you are sure that was what he said?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll take my oath to it, because I said to myself, when I heard it, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwonder what you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to pay for that little lot, for the District\r\nRailway Station\xe2\x80\x99s outside the four-mile radius.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs we drove off I was inclined to ask myself, a little bitterly--and\r\nperhaps unjustly--if it were not characteristic of the average London\r\npoliceman to almost forget the most important part of his\r\ninformation,--at any rate to leave it to the last and only to bring it\r\nto the front on having his palm crossed with silver.\r\n\r\nAs the hansom bowled along we three had what occasionally approached a\r\nwarm discussion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Marjorie was in that bundle,\xe2\x80\x99 began Lessingham, in the most lugubrious\r\nof tones, and with the most woebegone of faces.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I doubt it,\xe2\x80\x99 I observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She was,--I feel it,--I know it. She was either dead and mutilated, or\r\ngagged and drugged and helpless. All that remains is vengeance.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I repeat that I doubt it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAtherton struck in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am bound to say, with the best will in the world to think otherwise,\r\nthat I agree with Lessingham.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are wrong.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s all very well for you to talk in that cocksure way, but it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neasier for you to say I\xe2\x80\x99m wrong than to prove it. If I am wrong, and if\r\nLessingham\xe2\x80\x99s wrong, how do you explain his extraordinary insistence on\r\ntaking it inside the cab with him, which the bobby describes? If there\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t something horrible, awful in that bundle of his, of which he\r\nfeared the discovery, why was he so reluctant to have it placed upon\r\nthe roof?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There probably was something in it which he was particularly anxious\r\nshould not be discovered, but I doubt if it was anything of the kind\r\nwhich you suggest.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here is Marjorie in a house alone--nothing has been seen of her\r\nsince,--her clothing, her hair, is found hidden away under the floor.\r\nThis scoundrel sallies forth with a huge bundle on his head,--the bobby\r\nspeaks of it being five or six feet long, or longer,--a bundle which he\r\nregards with so much solicitude that he insists on never allowing it to\r\ngo, for a single instant, out of his sight and reach. What is in the\r\nthing? don\xe2\x80\x99t all the facts most unfortunately point in one direction?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr Lessingham covered his face with his hands, and groaned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I fear that Mr Atherton is right.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I differ from you both.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSydney at once became heated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then perhaps you can tell us what was in the bundle?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I fancy I could make a guess at the contents.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh you could, could you, then, perhaps, for our sakes, you\xe2\x80\x99ll make\r\nit,--and not play the oracular owl!--Lessingham and I are interested in\r\nthis business, after all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It contained the bearer\xe2\x80\x99s personal property: that, and nothing more.\r\nStay! before you jeer at me, suffer me to finish. If I am not mistaken\r\nas to the identity of the person whom the constable describes as the\r\nArab, I apprehend that the contents of that bundle were of much more\r\nimportance to him than if they had consisted of Miss Lindon, either\r\ndead or living. More. I am inclined to suspect that if the bundle was\r\nplaced on the roof of the cab, and if the driver did meddle with it,\r\nand did find out the contents, and understand them, he would have been\r\ndriven, out of hand, stark staring mad.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSydney was silent, as if he reflected. I imagine he perceived there was\r\nsomething in what I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But what has become of Miss Lindon?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I fancy that Miss Lindon, at this moment, is--somewhere; I don\xe2\x80\x99t, just\r\nnow, know exactly where, but I hope very shortly to be able to give you\r\na clearer notion,--attired in a rotten, dirty pair of boots; a filthy,\r\ntattered pair of trousers; a ragged, unwashed apology for a shirt; a\r\ngreasy, ancient, shapeless coat; and a frowsy peaked cloth cap.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey stared at me, opened-eyed. Atherton was the first to speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What on earth do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I mean that it seems to me that the facts point in the direction of my\r\nconclusions rather than yours--and that very strongly too. Miss Coleman\r\nasserts that she saw Miss Lindon return into the house; that within a\r\nfew minutes the blind was replaced at the front window; and that\r\nshortly after a young man, attired in the costume I have described,\r\ncame walking out of the front door. I believe that young man was Miss\r\nMarjorie Lindon.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLessingham and Atherton both broke out into interrogations, with\r\nSydney, as usual, loudest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But--man alive! what on earth should make her do a thing like that?\r\nMarjorie, the most retiring, modest girl on all God\xe2\x80\x99s earth, walk about\r\nin broad daylight, in such a costume, and for no reason at all! my dear\r\nChampnell, you are suggesting that she first of all went mad.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She was in a state of trance.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good God!--Champnell!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then you think that--juggling villain did get hold of her?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Undoubtedly. Here is my view of the case, mind it is only a hypothesis\r\nand you must take it for what it is worth. It seems to me quite clear\r\nthat the Arab, as we will call the person for the sake of\r\nidentification, was somewhere about the premises when you thought he\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But--where? We looked upstairs, and downstairs, and everywhere--where\r\ncould he have been?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That, as at present advised, I am not prepared to say, but I think you\r\nmay take it for granted that he was there. He hypnotised the man Holt,\r\nand sent him away, intending you to go after him, and so being rid of\r\nyou both--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The deuce he did, Champnell! You write me down an ass!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As soon as the coast was clear he discovered himself to Miss Lindon,\r\nwho, I expect, was disagreeably surprised, and hypnotised her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The hound!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The devil!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe first exclamation was Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s, the second Sydney\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He then constrained her to strip herself to the skin--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The wretch!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The fiend!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He cut off her hair; he hid it and her clothes under the floor where\r\nwe found them--where I think it probable that he had already some\r\nancient masculine garments concealed--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98By Jove! I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised if they were Holt\xe2\x80\x99s. I remember the\r\nman saying that that nice joker stripped him of his duds,--and\r\ncertainly when I saw him,--and when Marjorie found him!--he had\r\nabsolutely nothing on but a queer sort of cloak. Can it be possible\r\nthat that humorous professor of hankey-pankey--may all the maledictions\r\nof the accursed alight upon his head!--can have sent Marjorie Lindon,\r\nthe daintiest damsel in the land!--into the streets of London rigged\r\nout in Holt\xe2\x80\x99s old togs!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As to that, I am not able to give an authoritative opinion, but, if I\r\nunderstand you aright, it at least is possible. Anyhow I am disposed to\r\nthink that he sent Miss Lindon after the man Holt, taking it for\r\ngranted that he had eluded you.--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s it. Write me down an ass again!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That he did elude you, you have yourself admitted.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s because I stopped talking with that mutton-headed bobby,--I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nhave followed the man to the ends of the earth if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Precisely; the reason is immaterial, it is the fact with which we are\r\nimmediately concerned. He did elude you. And I think you will find that\r\nMiss Lindon and Mr Holt are together at this moment.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In men\xe2\x80\x99s clothing?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Both in men\xe2\x80\x99s clothing, or, rather, Miss Lindon is in a man\xe2\x80\x99s rags.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Great Potiphar! To think of Marjorie like that!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And where they are, the Arab is not very far off either.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLessingham caught me by the arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And what diabolical mischief do you imagine that he proposes to do to\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI shirked the question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whatever it is, it is our business to prevent his doing it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And where do you think they have been taken?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That it will be our immediate business to endeavour to discover,--and\r\nhere, at any rate, we are at Waterloo.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XLII.\r\n THE QUARRY DOUBLES\r\n\r\nI turned towards the booking-office on the main departure platform. As\r\nI went, the chief platform inspector, George Bellingham, with whom I\r\nhad some acquaintance, came out of his office. I stopped him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Bellingham, will you be so good as to step with me to the\r\nbooking-office, and instruct the clerk in charge to answer one or two\r\nquestions which I wish to put to him. I will explain to you afterwards\r\nwhat is their exact import, but you know me sufficiently to be able to\r\nbelieve me when I say that they refer to a matter in which every moment\r\nis of the first importance.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe turned and accompanied us into the interior of the booking-case.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To which of the clerks, Mr Champnell, do you wish to put your\r\nquestions?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To the one who issues third-class tickets to Southampton.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBellingham beckoned to a man who was counting a heap of money, and\r\napparently seeking to make it tally with the entries in a huge ledger\r\nwhich lay open before him,--he was a short, slightly-built young\r\nfellow, with a pleasant face and smiling eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Stone, this gentleman wishes to ask you one or two questions.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am at his service.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI put my questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I want to know, Mr Stone, if, in the course of the day, you have\r\nissued any tickets to a person dressed in Arab costume?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHis reply was prompt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have--by the last train, the 7.25,--three singles.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThree singles! Then my instinct had told me rightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Can you describe the person?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr Stone\xe2\x80\x99s eyes twinkled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I can, except in a general way,--he was uncommonly\r\nold and uncommonly ugly, and he had a pair of the most extraordinary\r\neyes I ever saw,--they gave me a sort of all-overish feeling when I saw\r\nthem glaring at me through the pigeon hole. But I can tell you one\r\nthing about him, he had a great bundle on his head, which he steadied\r\nwith one hand, and as it bulged out in all directions its presence\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t make him popular with other people who wanted tickets too.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nUndoubtedly this was our man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are sure he asked for three tickets?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Certain. He said three tickets to Southampton; laid down the exact\r\nfare,--nineteen and six--and held up three fingers--like that. Three\r\nnasty looking fingers they were, with nails as long as talons.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You didn\xe2\x80\x99t see who were his companions?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I didn\xe2\x80\x99t,--I didn\xe2\x80\x99t try to look. I gave him his tickets and off he\r\nwent,--with the people grumbling at him because that bundle of his kept\r\ngetting in their way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBellingham touched me on the arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I can tell you about the Arab of whom Mr Stone speaks. My attention\r\nwas called to him by his insisting on taking his bundle with him into\r\nthe carriage,--it was an enormous thing, he could hardly squeeze it\r\nthrough the door; it occupied the entire seat. But as there weren\xe2\x80\x99t as\r\nmany passengers as usual, and he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t or couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be made to\r\nunderstand that his precious bundle would be safe in the luggage van\r\nalong with the rest of the luggage, and as he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the sort of person\r\nyou could argue with to any advantage, I had him put into an empty\r\ncompartment, bundle and all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Was he alone then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought so at the time, he said nothing about having more than one\r\nticket, or any companions, but just before the train started two other\r\nmen--English men--got into his compartment; and as I came down the\r\nplatform, the ticket inspector at the barrier informed me that these\r\ntwo men were with him, because he held tickets for the three, which, as\r\nhe was a foreigner, and they seemed English, struck the inspector as\r\nodd.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Could you describe the two men?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, not particularly, but the man who had charge of the\r\nbarrier might. I was at the other end of the train when they got in.\r\nAll I noticed was that one seemed to be a commonplace looking\r\nindividual and that the other was dressed like a tramp, all rags and\r\ntatters, a disreputable looking object he appeared to be.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That,\xe2\x80\x99 I said to myself, \xe2\x80\x98was Miss Marjorie Lindon, the lovely\r\ndaughter of a famous house; the wife-elect of a coming statesman.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTo Bellingham I remarked aloud:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I want you to strain a point, Mr Bellingham, and to do me a service\r\nwhich I assure you you shall never have any cause to regret. I want you\r\nto wire instructions down the line to detain this Arab and his\r\ncompanions and to keep them in custody until the receipt of further\r\ninstructions. They are not wanted by the police as yet, but they will\r\nbe as soon as I am able to give certain information to the authorities\r\nat Scotland Yard,--and wanted very badly. But, as you will perceive for\r\nyourself, until I am able to give that information every moment is\r\nimportant.--Where\xe2\x80\x99s the Station Superintendent?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He\xe2\x80\x99s gone. At present I\xe2\x80\x99m in charge.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then will you do this for me? I repeat that you shall never have any\r\nreason to regret it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will if you\xe2\x80\x99ll accept all responsibility.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll do that with the greatest pleasure.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBellingham looked at his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s about twenty minutes to nine. The train\xe2\x80\x99s scheduled for\r\nBasingstoke at 9.6. If we wire to Basingstoke at once they ought to be\r\nready for them when they come.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe wire was sent.\r\n\r\nWe were shown into Bellingham\xe2\x80\x99s office to await results. Lessingham\r\npaced agitatedly to and fro; he seemed to have reached the limits of\r\nhis self-control, and to be in a condition in which movement of some\r\nsort was an absolute necessity. The mercurial Sydney, on the contrary,\r\nleaned back in a chair, his legs stretched out in front of him, his\r\nhands thrust deep into his trouser pockets, and stared at Lessingham,\r\nas if he found relief to his feelings in watching his companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrestlessness. I, for my part, drew up as full a pr\xc3\xa9cis of the case as I\r\ndeemed advisable, and as time permitted, which I despatched by one of\r\nthe company\xe2\x80\x99s police to Scotland Yard.\r\n\r\nThen I turned to my associates.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, gentlemen, it\xe2\x80\x99s past dinner time. We may have a journey in front\r\nof us. If you take my advice you\xe2\x80\x99ll have something to eat.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLessingham shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I want nothing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nor I,\xe2\x80\x99 echoed Sydney.\r\n\r\nI started up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You must pardon my saying nonsense, but surely you of all men, Mr\r\nLessingham, should be aware that you will not improve the situation by\r\nrendering yourself incapable of seeing it through. Come and dine.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI haled them off with me, willy nilly, to the refreshment room. I\r\ndined,--after a fashion; Mr Lessingham swallowed with difficulty, a\r\nplate of soup; Sydney nibbled at a plate of the most unpromising\r\nlooking \xe2\x80\x98chicken and ham,\xe2\x80\x99--he proved, indeed, more intractable than\r\nLessingham, and was not to be persuaded to tackle anything easier of\r\ndigestion.\r\n\r\nI was just about to take cheese after chop when Bellingham came\r\nhastening in, in his hand an open telegram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The birds have flown,\xe2\x80\x99 he cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Flown!--How?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIn reply he gave me the telegram. I glanced at it. It ran:\r\n\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x98Persons described not in the train. Guard says they got out at\r\n Vauxhall. Have wired Vauxhall to advise you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s a level-headed chap,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bellingham. \xe2\x80\x98The man who sent that\r\ntelegram. His wiring to Vauxhall should save us a lot of time,--we\r\nought to hear from there directly. Hollo! what\xe2\x80\x99s this? I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nsurprised if this is it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs he spoke a porter entered,--he handed an envelope to Bellingham. We\r\nall three kept our eyes fixed on the inspector\xe2\x80\x99s face as he opened it.\r\nWhen he perceived the contents he gave an exclamation of surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This Arab of yours, and his two friends, seem rather a curious lot, Mr\r\nChampnell.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe passed the paper on to me. It took the form of a report. Lessingham\r\nand Sydney, regardless of forms and ceremonies, leaned over my shoulder\r\nas I read it.\r\n\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x98Passengers by 7.30 Southampton, on arrival of train, complained of\r\n noises coming from a compartment in coach 8964. Stated that there had\r\n been shrieks and yells ever since the train left Waterloo, as if\r\n someone was being murdered. An Arab and two Englishmen got out of the\r\n compartment in question, apparently the party referred to in wire just\r\n to hand from Basingstoke. All three declared that there was nothing the\r\n matter. That they had been shouting for fun. Arab gave up three third\r\n singles for Southampton, saying, in reply to questions, that they had\r\n changed their minds, and did not want to go any farther. As there were\r\n no signs of a struggle or of violence, nor, apparently, any definite\r\n cause for detention, they were allowed to pass. They took a\r\n four-wheeler, No. 09435. The Arab and one man went inside, and the\r\n other man on the box. They asked to be driven to Commercial Road,\r\n Limehouse. The cab has since returned. Driver says he put the three men\r\n down, at their request, in Commercial Road, at the corner of Sutcliffe\r\n Street, near the East India Docks. They walked up Sutcliffe Street, the\r\n Englishmen in front, and the Arab behind, took the first turning to the\r\n right, and after that he saw nothing of them. The driver further states\r\n that all the way the Englishman inside, who was so ragged and dirty\r\n that he was reluctant to carry him, kept up a sort of wailing noise\r\n which so attracted his attention that he twice got off his box to see\r\n what was the matter, and each time he said it was nothing. The cabman\r\n is of opinion that both the Englishmen were of weak intellect. We were\r\n of the same impression here. They said nothing, except at the seeming\r\n instigation of the Arab, but when spoken to stared and gaped like\r\n lunatics.\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x98It may be mentioned that the Arab had with him an enormous bundle,\r\n which he persisted, in spite of all remonstrances, on taking with him\r\n inside the cab.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\nAs soon as I had mastered the contents of the report, and perceived\r\nwhat I believed to be--unknown to the writer himself--its hideous inner\r\nmeaning, I turned to Bellingham.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98With your permission, Mr Bellingham, I will keep this\r\ncommunication,--it will be safe in my hands, you will be able to get a\r\ncopy, and it may be necessary that I should have the original to show\r\nto the police. If any inquiries are made for me from Scotland Yard,\r\ntell them that I have gone to the Commercial Road, and that I will\r\nreport my movements from Limehouse Police Station.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIn another minute we were once more traversing the streets of\r\nLondon,--three in a hansom cab.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XLIII.\r\n THE MURDER AT MRS \xe2\x80\x99ENDERSON\xe2\x80\x99S\r\n\r\nIt is something of a drive from Waterloo to Limehouse,--it seems longer\r\nwhen all your nerves are tingling with anxiety to reach your journey\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nend; and the cab I had hit upon proved to be not the fastest I might\r\nhave chosen. For some time after our start, we were silent. Each was\r\noccupied with his own thoughts.\r\n\r\nThen Lessingham, who was sitting at my side, said to me,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Champnell, you have that report.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Will you let me see it once more?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI gave it to him. He read it once, twice,--and I fancy yet again. I\r\npurposely avoided looking at him as he did so. Yet all the while I was\r\nconscious of his pallid cheeks, the twitched muscles of his mouth, the\r\nfeverish glitter of his eyes,--this Leader of Men, whose predominate\r\ncharacteristic in the House of Commons was immobility, was rapidly\r\napproximating to the condition of a hysterical woman. The mental strain\r\nwhich he had been recently undergoing was proving too much for his\r\nphysical strength. This disappearance of the woman he loved bade fair\r\nto be the final straw. I felt convinced that unless something was done\r\nquickly to relieve the strain upon his mind he was nearer to a state of\r\ncomplete mental and moral collapse than he himself imagined. Had he\r\nbeen under my orders I should have commanded him to at once return\r\nhome, and not to think; but conscious that, as things were, such a\r\ndirection would be simply futile, I decided to do something else\r\ninstead. Feeling that suspense was for him the worst possible form of\r\nsuffering I resolved to explain, so far as I was able, precisely what\r\nit was I feared, and how I proposed to prevent it.\r\n\r\nPresently there came the question for which I had been waiting, in a\r\nharsh, broken voice which no one who had heard him speak on a public\r\nplatform, or in the House of Commons, would have recognised as his.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Champnell,--who do you think this person is of whom the report from\r\nVauxhall Station speaks as being all in rags and tatters?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe knew perfectly well,--but I understood the mental attitude which\r\ninduced him to prefer that the information should seem to come from me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope that it will prove to be Miss Lindon.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hope!\xe2\x80\x99 He gave a sort of gasp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, hope,--because if it is I think it possible, nay probable, that\r\nwithin a few hours you will have her again enfolded in your arms.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pray God that it may be so! pray God!--pray the good God!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI did not dare to look round for, from the tremor which was in his\r\ntone, I was persuaded that in the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were tears. Atherton\r\ncontinued silent. He was leaning half out of the cab, staring straight\r\nahead, as if he saw in front a young girl\xe2\x80\x99s face, from which he could\r\nnot remove his glance, and which beckoned him on.\r\n\r\nAfter a while Lessingham spoke again, as if half to himself and half to\r\nme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This mention of the shrieks on the railway, and of the wailing noise\r\nin the cab,--what must this wretch have done to her? How my darling\r\nmust have suffered!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThat was a theme on which I myself scarcely ventured to allow my\r\nthoughts to rest. The notion of a gently-nurtured girl being at the\r\nmercy of that fiend incarnate, possessed--as I believed that so-called\r\nArab to be possessed--of all the paraphernalia of horror and of dread,\r\nwas one which caused me tangible shrinkings of the body. Whence had\r\ncome those shrieks and yells, of which the writer of the report spoke,\r\nwhich had caused the Arab\xe2\x80\x99s fellow-passengers to think that murder was\r\nbeing done? What unimaginable agony had caused them? what speechless\r\ntorture? And the \xe2\x80\x98wailing noise,\xe2\x80\x99 which had induced the prosaic,\r\nindurated London cabman to get twice off his box to see what was the\r\nmatter, what anguish had been provocative of that? The helpless girl\r\nwho had already endured so much, endured, perhaps, that to which death\r\nwould have been preferred!--shut up in that rattling, jolting box on\r\nwheels, alone with that diabolical Asiatic, with the enormous bundle,\r\nwhich was but the lurking place of nameless terrors,--what might she\r\nnot, while being borne through the heart of civilised London, have been\r\nmade to suffer? What had she not been made to suffer to have kept up\r\nthat continued \xe2\x80\x98wailing noise\xe2\x80\x99?\r\n\r\nIt was not a theme on which it was wise to permit one\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts to\r\nlinger,--and particularly was it clear that it was one from which\r\nLessingham\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts should have been kept as far as possible away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come, Mr Lessingham, neither you nor I will do himself any good by\r\npermitting his reflections to flow in a morbid channel. Let us talk of\r\nsomething else. By the way, weren\xe2\x80\x99t you due to speak in the House\r\nto-night?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Due!--Yes, I was due,--but what does it matter?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But have you acquainted no one with the cause of your non-attendance?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Acquaint!--whom should I acquaint?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My good sir! Listen to me, Mr Lessingham. Let me entreat you very\r\nearnestly, to follow my advice. Call another cab,--or take this! and go\r\nat once to the House. It is not too late. Play the man, deliver the\r\nspeech you have undertaken to deliver, perform your political duties.\r\nBy coming with me you will be a hindrance rather than a help, and you\r\nmay do your reputation an injury from which it never may recover. Do as\r\nI counsel you, and I will undertake to do my very utmost to let you\r\nhave good news by the time your speech is finished.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe turned on me with a bitterness for which I was unprepared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If I were to go down to the House, and try to speak in the state in\r\nwhich I am now, they would laugh at me, I should be ruined.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you not run an equally great risk of being ruined by staying away?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe gripped me by the arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Champnell, do you know that I am on the verge of madness? Do you\r\nknow that as I am sitting here by your side I am living in a dual\r\nworld? I am going on and on to catch that--that fiend, and I am back\r\nagain in that Egyptian den, upon that couch of rugs, with the Woman of\r\nthe Songs beside me, and Marjorie is being torn and tortured, and burnt\r\nbefore my eyes! God help me! Her shrieks are ringing in my ears!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe did not speak loudly, but his voice was none the less impressive on\r\nthat account. I endeavoured my hardest to be stern.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I confess that you disappoint me, Mr Lessingham. I have always\r\nunderstood that you were a man of unusual strength; you appear instead,\r\nto be a man of extraordinary weakness; with an imagination so\r\nill-governed that its ebullitions remind me of nothing so much as\r\nfeminine hysterics. Your wild language is not warranted by\r\ncircumstances. I repeat that I think it quite possible that by\r\nto-morrow morning she will be returned to you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,--but how? as the Marjorie I have known, as I saw her last,--or\r\nhow?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThat was the question which I had already asked myself, in what\r\ncondition would she be when we had succeeded in snatching her from her\r\ncaptor\xe2\x80\x99s grip? It was a question to which I had refused to supply an\r\nanswer. To him I lied by implication.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Let us hope that, with the exception of being a trifle scared, she\r\nwill be as sound and hale and hearty as ever in her life.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you yourself believe that she\xe2\x80\x99ll be like that,--untouched,\r\nunchanged, unstained?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThen I lied right out,--it seemed to me necessary to calm his growing\r\nexcitement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr Lessingham!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you think that I can\xe2\x80\x99t see your face and read in it the same\r\nthoughts which trouble me? As a man of honour do you care to deny that\r\nwhen Marjorie Lindon is restored to me,--if she ever is!--you fear she\r\nwill be but the mere soiled husk of the Marjorie whom I knew and loved?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Even supposing that there may be a modicum of truth in what you\r\nsay,--which I am far from being disposed to admit--what good purpose do\r\nyou propose to serve by talking in such a strain?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98None,--no good purpose,--unless it be the desire of looking the truth\r\nin the face. For, Mr Champnell, you must not seek to play with me the\r\nhypocrite, nor try to hide things from me as if I were a child. If my\r\nlife is ruined--it is ruined,--let me know it, and look the knowledge\r\nin the face. That, to me, is to play the man.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI was silent.\r\n\r\nThe wild tale he had told me of that Cairene inferno, oddly enough--yet\r\nwhy oddly, for the world is all coincidence!--had thrown a flood of\r\nlight on certain events which had happened some three years previously\r\nand which ever since had remained shrouded in mystery. The conduct of\r\nthe business afterwards came into my hands,--and briefly, what had\r\noccurred was this:\r\n\r\nThree persons,--two sisters and their brother, who was younger than\r\nthemselves, members of a decent English family, were going on a trip\r\nround the world. They were young, adventurous, and--not to put too fine\r\na point on it--foolhardy. The evening after their arrival in Cairo, by\r\nway of what is called \xe2\x80\x98a lark,\xe2\x80\x99 in spite of the protestations of people\r\nwho were better informed than themselves, they insisted on going,\r\nalone, for a ramble through the native quarter.\r\n\r\nThey went,--but they never returned. Or, rather the two girls never\r\nreturned. After an interval the young man was found again,--what was\r\nleft of him. A fuss was made when there were no signs of their\r\nre-appearance, but as there were no relations, nor even friends of\r\ntheirs, but only casual acquaintances on board the ship by which they\r\nhad travelled, perhaps not so great a fuss as might have been was made.\r\nAnyhow, nothing was discovered. Their widowed mother, alone in England,\r\nwondering how it was that beyond the receipt of a brief wire,\r\nacquainting her with their arrival at Cairo, she had heard nothing\r\nfurther of their wanderings, placed herself in communication with the\r\ndiplomatic people over there,--to learn that, to all appearances, her\r\nthree children had vanished from off the face of the earth.\r\n\r\nThen a fuss was made,--with a vengeance. So far as one can judge the\r\nwhole town and neighbourhood was turned pretty well upside down. But\r\nnothing came of it,--so far as any results were concerned, the\r\nauthorities might just as well have left the mystery of their\r\nvanishment alone. It continued where it was in spite of them.\r\n\r\nHowever, some three months afterwards a youth was brought to the\r\nBritish Embassy by a party of friendly Arabs who asserted that they had\r\nfound him naked and nearly dying in some remote spot in the Wady Halfa\r\ndesert. It was the brother of the two lost girls. He was as nearly\r\ndying as he very well could be without being actually dead when they\r\nbrought him to the Embassy,--and in a state of indescribable\r\nmutilation. He seemed to rally for a time under careful treatment, but\r\nhe never again uttered a coherent word. It was only from his delirious\r\nravings that any idea was formed of what had really occurred.\r\n\r\nShorthand notes were taken of some of the utterances of his delirium.\r\nAfterwards they were submitted to me. I remembered the substance of\r\nthem quite well, and when Mr Lessingham began to tell me of his own\r\nhideous experiences they came back to me more clearly still. Had I laid\r\nthose notes before him I have little doubt but that he would have\r\nimmediately perceived that seventeen years after the adventure which\r\nhad left such an indelible scar upon his own life, this youth--he was\r\nlittle more than a boy--had seen the things which he had seen, and\r\nsuffered the nameless agonies and degradations which he had suffered.\r\nThe young man was perpetually raving about some indescribable den of\r\nhorror which was own brother to Lessingham\xe2\x80\x99s temple and about some\r\nfemale monster, whom he regarded with such fear and horror that every\r\nallusion he made to her was followed by a convulsive paroxysm which\r\ntaxed all the ingenuity of his medical attendants to bring him out of.\r\nHe frequently called upon his sisters by name, speaking of them in a\r\nmanner which inevitably suggested that he had been an unwilling and\r\nhelpless witness of hideous tortures which they had undergone; and then\r\nhe would rise in bed, screaming, \xe2\x80\x98They\xe2\x80\x99re burning them! they\xe2\x80\x99re burning\r\nthem! Devils! devils!\xe2\x80\x99 And at those times it required all the strength\r\nof those who were in attendance to restrain his maddened frenzy.\r\n\r\nThe youth died in one of these fits of great preternatural excitement,\r\nwithout, as I have previously written, having given utterance to one\r\nsingle coherent word, and by some of those who were best able to judge\r\nit was held to have been a mercy that he did die without having been\r\nrestored to consciousness. And, presently, tales began to be whispered,\r\nabout some idolatrous sect, which was stated to have its headquarters\r\nsomewhere in the interior of the country--some located it in this\r\nneighbourhood, and some in that--which was stated to still practise,\r\nand to always have practised, in unbroken historical continuity, the\r\ndebased, unclean, mystic, and bloody rites, of a form of idolatry which\r\nhad had its birth in a period of the world\xe2\x80\x99s story which was so remote,\r\nthat to all intents and purposes it might be described as pre-historic.\r\n\r\nWhile the ferment was still at its height, a man came to the British\r\nEmbassy who said that he was a member of a tribe which had its habitat\r\non the banks of the White Nile. He asserted that he was in association\r\nwith this very idolatrous sect,--though he denied that he was one of\r\nthe actual sectaries. He did admit, however, that he had assisted more\r\nthan once at their orgies, and declared that it was their constant\r\npractice to offer young women as sacrifices--preferably white Christian\r\nwomen, with a special preference, if they could get them, to young\r\nEnglish women. He vowed that he himself had seen with his own eyes,\r\nEnglish girls burnt alive. The description which he gave of what\r\npreceded and followed these foul murders appalled those who listened.\r\nHe finally wound up by offering, on payment of a stipulated sum of\r\nmoney, to guide a troop of soldiers to this den of demons, so that they\r\nshould arrive there at a moment when it was filled with worshippers,\r\nwho were preparing to participate in an orgie which was to take place\r\nduring the next few days.\r\n\r\nHis offer was conditionally accepted. He was confined in an apartment\r\nwith one man on guard inside and another on guard outside the room.\r\nThat night the sentinel without was startled by hearing a great noise\r\nand frightful screams issuing from the chamber in which the native was\r\ninterned. He summoned assistance. The door was opened. The soldier on\r\nguard within was stark, staring mad,--he died within a few months, a\r\ngibbering maniac to the end. The native was dead. The window, which was\r\na very small one, was securely fastened inside and strongly barred\r\nwithout. There was nothing to show by what means entry had been gained.\r\nYet it was the general opinion of those who saw the corpse that the man\r\nhad been destroyed by some wild beast. A photograph was taken of the\r\nbody after death, a copy of which is still in my possession. In it are\r\ndistinctly shown lacerations about the neck and the lower portion of\r\nthe abdomen, as if they had been produced by the claws of some huge and\r\nferocious animal. The skull is splintered in half-a-dozen places, and\r\nthe face is torn to rags.\r\n\r\nThat was more than three years ago. The whole business has remained as\r\ngreat a mystery as ever. But my attention has once or twice been caught\r\nby trifling incidents, which have caused me to more than suspect that\r\nthe wild tale told by that murdered native had in it at least the\r\nelements of truth; and which have even led me to wonder if the trade in\r\nkidnapping was not being carried on to this very hour, and if women of\r\nmy own flesh and blood were not still being offered up on that infernal\r\naltar. And now, here was Paul Lessingham, a man of world-wide\r\nreputation, of great intellect, of undoubted honour, who had come to me\r\nwith a wholly unconscious verification of all my worst suspicions!\r\n\r\nThat the creature spoken of as an Arab,--and who was probably no more\r\nan Arab than I was, and whose name was certainly not Mohamed el\r\nKheir!--was an emissary from that den of demons, I had no doubt. What\r\nwas the exact purport of the creature\xe2\x80\x99s presence in England was another\r\nquestion. Possibly part of the intention was the destruction of Paul\r\nLessingham, body, soul and spirit; possibly another part was the\r\nprocuration of fresh victims for that long-drawn-out holocaust. That\r\nthis latter object explained the disappearance of Miss Lindon I felt\r\npersuaded. That she was designed by the personification of evil who was\r\nher captor, to suffer all the horrors at which the stories pointed, and\r\nthen to be burned alive, amidst the triumphant yells of the attendant\r\ndemons, I was certain. That the wretch, aware that the pursuit was in\r\nfull cry, was tearing, twisting, doubling, and would stick at nothing\r\nwhich would facilitate the smuggling of the victim out of England, was\r\nclear.\r\n\r\nMy interest in the quest was already far other than a merely\r\nprofessional one. The blood in my veins tingled at the thought of such\r\na woman as Miss Lindon being in the power of such a monster. I may\r\nassuredly claim that throughout the whole business I was urged forward\r\nby no thought of fee or of reward. To have had a share in rescuing that\r\nunfortunate girl, and in the destruction of her noxious persecutor,\r\nwould have been reward enough for me.\r\n\r\nOne is not always, even in strictly professional matters, influenced by\r\nstrictly professional instincts.\r\n\r\nThe cab slowed. A voice descended through the trap door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This is Commercial Road, sir,--what part of it do you want?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Drive me to Limehouse Police Station.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWe were driven there. I made my way to the usual inspector behind the\r\nusual pigeon-hole.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My name is Champnell. Have you received any communication from\r\nScotland Yard to-night having reference to a matter in which I am\r\ninterested?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you mean about the Arab? We received a telephonic message about\r\nhalf an hour ago.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Since communicating with Scotland Yard this has come to hand from the\r\nauthorities at Vauxhall Station. Can you tell me if anything has been\r\nseen of the person in question by the men of your division?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI handed the Inspector the \xe2\x80\x98report.\xe2\x80\x99 His reply was laconic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will inquire.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe passed through a door into an inner room and the \xe2\x80\x98report\xe2\x80\x99 went with\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Beg pardon, sir, but was that a Harab you was a-talking about to the\r\nHinspector?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe speaker was a gentleman unmistakably of the guttersnipe class. He\r\nwas seated on a form. Close at hand hovered a policeman whose special\r\nduty it seemed to be to keep an eye upon his movements.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why do you ask?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I beg your pardon, sir, but I saw a Harab myself about a hour\r\nago,--leastways he looked like as if he was a Harab.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What sort of a looking person was he?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I can\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ardly tell you that, sir, because I didn\xe2\x80\x99t never have a\r\nproper look at him,--but I know he had a bloomin\xe2\x80\x99 great bundle on \xe2\x80\x99is\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ead.\xe2\x80\xa6 It was like this, \xe2\x80\x99ere. I was comin\xe2\x80\x99 round the corner, as he\r\nwas passin\xe2\x80\x99, I never see \xe2\x80\x99im till I was right atop of \xe2\x80\x99im, so that I\r\nhaccidentally run agin \xe2\x80\x99im,--my heye! didn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99e give me a downer! I was\r\ndown on the back of my \xe2\x80\x99ead in the middle of the road before I knew\r\nwhere I was and \xe2\x80\x99e was at the other end of the street. If \xe2\x80\x99e \xe2\x80\x99adn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknocked me more\xe2\x80\x99n \xe2\x80\x99arf silly I\xe2\x80\x99d been after \xe2\x80\x99im, sharp,--I tell you!\r\nand hasked \xe2\x80\x99im what \xe2\x80\x99e thought \xe2\x80\x99e was a-doin\xe2\x80\x99 of, but afore my senses\r\nwas back agin \xe2\x80\x99e was out o\xe2\x80\x99 sight,--clean!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are sure he had a bundle on his head?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I noticed it most particular.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How long ago do you say this was? and where?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98About a hour ago,--perhaps more, perhaps less.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Was he alone?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It seemed to me as if a cove was a follerin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99im, leastways there was\r\na bloke as was a-keepin\xe2\x80\x99 close at \xe2\x80\x99is \xe2\x80\x99eels,--though I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\n\xe2\x80\x99is little game was, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure. Ask the pleesman--he knows, he knows\r\neverythink, the pleesman do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI turned to the \xe2\x80\x98pleesman.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who is this man?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x98pleesman\xe2\x80\x99 put his hands behind his back, and threw out his chest.\r\nHis manner was distinctly affable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well,--he\xe2\x80\x99s being detained upon suspicion. I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbe particular about a lie or two.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThis frank expression of opinion re-aroused the indignation of the\r\ngentleman on the form.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There you hare! at it again! That\xe2\x80\x99s just like you peelers,--you\xe2\x80\x99re all\r\nthe same! What do you know about me?--Nuffink! This gen\xe2\x80\x99leman ain\xe2\x80\x99t got\r\nno call to believe me, not as I knows on,--it\xe2\x80\x99s all the same to me if\r\n\xe2\x80\x99e do or don\xe2\x80\x99t, but it\xe2\x80\x99s trewth what I\xe2\x80\x99m sayin\xe2\x80\x99, all the same.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAt this point the Inspector re-appeared at the pigeon-hole. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want none of your \xe2\x80\x99andling!--I ain\xe2\x80\x99t done\r\nnuffink to you! I tell you \xe2\x80\x99e \xe2\x80\x99as!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe Inspector spoke through the pigeon-hole.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has what, my lad? What do you say has happened?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s been murder done--it\xe2\x80\x99s right enough!--there \xe2\x80\x99as!--up at Mrs\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Enderson\xe2\x80\x99s, in Paradise Place,--a Harab\xe2\x80\x99s been and killed a bloke!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n CHAPTER XLIV.\r\n THE MAN WHO WAS MURDERED\r\n\r\nThe Inspector spoke to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If what the boy says is correct it sounds as if the person whom you\r\nare seeking may have had a finger in the pie.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI was of the same opinion, as, apparently, were Lessingham and Sydney.\r\nAtherton collared the youth by the shoulder which Mr Pleesman had left\r\ndisengaged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What sort of looking bloke is it who\xe2\x80\x99s been murdered?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I dunno! I \xe2\x80\x99aven\xe2\x80\x99t seen \xe2\x80\x99im! Mrs \xe2\x80\x99Enderson, she says to me! \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Gustus\r\nBarley,\xe2\x80\x9d she says, \xe2\x80\x9ca bloke\xe2\x80\x99s been murdered. That there Harab what I\r\nchucked out \xe2\x80\x99alf a hour ago been and murdered \xe2\x80\x99im, and left \xe2\x80\x99im behind\r\nup in my back room. You run as \xe2\x80\x99ard as you can tear and tell them there\r\ndratted pleese what\xe2\x80\x99s so fond of shovin\xe2\x80\x99 their dirty noses into\r\nrespectable people\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99ouses.\xe2\x80\x9d So I comes and tells yer. That\xe2\x80\x99s all I\r\nknows about it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWe went four in the hansom which had been waiting in the street to Mrs\r\nHenderson\xe2\x80\x99s in Paradise Place,--the Inspector and we three. \xe2\x80\x98Mr\r\nPleesman\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Gustus Barley\xe2\x80\x99 followed on foot. The Inspector was\r\nexplanatory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs Henderson keeps a sort of lodging-house,--a \xe2\x80\x9cSailors\xe2\x80\x99 Home\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ncalls it, but no one could call it sweet. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t bear the best of\r\ncharacters, and if you asked me what I thought of it, I should say in\r\nplain English that it was a disorderly house.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nParadise Place proved to be within three or four hundred yards of the\r\nStation House. So far as could be seen in the dark it consisted of a\r\nrow of houses of considerable dimensions,--and also of considerable\r\nantiquity. They opened on to two or three stone steps which led\r\ndirectly into the street. At one of the doors stood an old lady with a\r\nshawl drawn over her head. This was Mrs Henderson. She greeted us with\r\ngarrulous volubility.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So you \xe2\x80\x99ave come, \xe2\x80\x99ave you? I thought you never was a-comin\xe2\x80\x99 that I\r\ndid.\xe2\x80\x99 She recognised the Inspector. \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s you, Mr Phillips, is it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nPerceiving us, she drew a little back. \xe2\x80\x98Who\xe2\x80\x99s them \xe2\x80\x99ere parties? They\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t coppers?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr Phillips dismissed her inquiry, curtly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Never you mind who they are. What\xe2\x80\x99s this about someone being murdered.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ssh!\xe2\x80\x99 The old lady glanced round. \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t you speak so loud, Mr\r\nPhillips. No one don\xe2\x80\x99t know nothing about it as yet. The parties what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nin my \xe2\x80\x99ouse is most respectable,--most! and they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t abide the\r\nnotion of there being police about the place.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We quite believe that, Mrs Henderson.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe Inspector\xe2\x80\x99s tone was grim.\r\n\r\nMrs Henderson led the way up a staircase which would have been\r\ndistinctly the better for repairs. It was necessary to pick one\xe2\x80\x99s way\r\nas one went, and as the light was defective stumbles were not\r\ninfrequent.\r\n\r\nOur guide paused outside a door on the topmost landing. From some\r\nmysterious recess in her apparel she produced a key.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s in \xe2\x80\x99ere. I locked the door so that nothing mightn\xe2\x80\x99t be disturbed.\r\nI knows \xe2\x80\x99ow particular you pleesmen is.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe turned the key. We all went in--we, this time, in front, and she\r\nbehind.\r\n\r\nA candle was guttering on a broken and dilapidated single washhand\r\nstand. A small iron bedstead stood by its side, the clothes on which\r\nwere all tumbled and tossed. There was a rush-seated chair with a hole\r\nin the seat,--and that, with the exception of one or two chipped pieces\r\nof stoneware, and a small round mirror which was hung on a nail against\r\nthe wall, seemed to be all that the room contained. I could see nothing\r\nin the shape of a murdered man. Nor, it appeared, could the Inspector\r\neither.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s the meaning of this, Mrs Henderson? I don\xe2\x80\x99t see anything here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s be\xe2\x80\x99ind the bed, Mr Phillips. I left \xe2\x80\x99im just where I found \xe2\x80\x99im, I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ave touched \xe2\x80\x99im not for nothing, nor yet \xe2\x80\x99ave let nobody else\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ave touched \xe2\x80\x99im neither, because, as I say, I know \xe2\x80\x99ow particular you\r\npleesmen is.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWe all four went hastily forward. Atherton and I went to the head of\r\nthe bed, Lessingham and the Inspector, leaning right across the bed,\r\npeeped over the side. There, on the floor in the space which was\r\nbetween the bed and the wall, lay the murdered man.\r\n\r\nAt sight of him an exclamation burst from Sydney\xe2\x80\x99s lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s Holt!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank God!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Lessingham. \xe2\x80\x98It isn\xe2\x80\x99t Marjorie!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe relief in his tone was unmistakable. That the one was gone was\r\nplainly nothing to him in comparison with the fact that the other was\r\nleft.\r\n\r\nThrusting the bed more into the centre of the room I knelt down beside\r\nthe man on the floor. A more deplorable spectacle than he presented I\r\nhave seldom witnessed. He was decently clad in a grey tweed suit, white\r\nhat, collar and necktie, and it was perhaps that fact which made his\r\nextreme attenuation the more conspicuous. I doubt if there was an ounce\r\nof flesh on the whole of his body. His cheeks and the sockets of his\r\neyes were hollow. The skin was drawn tightly over his cheek bones,--the\r\nbones themselves were staring through. Even his nose was wasted, so\r\nthat nothing but a ridge of cartilage remained. I put my arm beneath\r\nhis shoulder and raised him from the floor; no resistance was offered\r\nby the body\xe2\x80\x99s gravity,--he was as light as a little child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I doubt,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98if this man has been murdered. It looks to me like\r\na case of starvation, or exhaustion,--possibly a combination of both.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s that on his neck?\xe2\x80\x99 asked the Inspector,--he was kneeling at my\r\nside.\r\n\r\nHe referred to two abrasions of the skin,--one on either side of the\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s neck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They look to me like scratches. They seem pretty deep, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink they\xe2\x80\x99re sufficient in themselves to cause death.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They might be, joined to an already weakened constitution. Is there\r\nanything in his pockets?--let\xe2\x80\x99s lift him on to the bed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWe lifted him on to the bed,--a featherweight he was to lift. While the\r\nInspector was examining his pockets--to find them empty--a tall man\r\nwith a big black beard came bustling in. He proved to be Dr Glossop,\r\nthe local police surgeon, who had been sent for before our quitting the\r\nStation House.\r\n\r\nHis first pronouncement, made as soon as he commenced his examination,\r\nwas, under the circumstances, sufficiently startling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe the man\xe2\x80\x99s dead. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you send for me directly\r\nyou found him?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe question was put to Mrs Henderson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, Dr Glossop, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t touch \xe2\x80\x99im myself, and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ave \xe2\x80\x99im\r\ntouched by no one else, because, as I\xe2\x80\x99ve said afore, I know \xe2\x80\x99ow\r\nparticular them pleesmen is.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then in that case, if he does die you\xe2\x80\x99ll have had a hand in murdering\r\nhim,--that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe lady sniggered. \xe2\x80\x98Of course Dr Glossop, we all knows that you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nalways \xe2\x80\x99ave your joke.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99ll find it a joke if you have to hang, as you ought to, you----\xe2\x80\x99\r\nThe doctor said what he did say to himself, under his breath. I doubt\r\nif it was flattering to Mrs Henderson. \xe2\x80\x98Have you got any brandy in the\r\nhouse?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99ve got everythink in the \xe2\x80\x99ouse for them as likes to pay for\r\nit,--everythink.\xe2\x80\x99 Then, suddenly remembering that the police were\r\npresent, and that hers were not exactly licensed premises, \xe2\x80\x98Leastways\r\nwe can send out for it for them parties as gives us the money, being,\r\nas is well known, always willing to oblige.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then send for some,--to the tap downstairs, if that\xe2\x80\x99s the nearest! If\r\nthis man dies before you\xe2\x80\x99ve brought it I\xe2\x80\x99ll have you locked up as sure\r\nas you\xe2\x80\x99re a living woman.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe arrival of the brandy was not long delayed,--but the man on the bed\r\nhad regained consciousness before it came. Opening his eyes he looked\r\nup at the doctor bending over him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hollo, my man! that\xe2\x80\x99s more like the time of day! How are you feeling?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe patient stared hazily up at the doctor, as if his sense of\r\nperception was not yet completely restored,--as if this big bearded man\r\nwas something altogether strange. Atherton bent down beside the doctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m glad to see you looking better, Mr Holt. You know me don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been running about after you all day long.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are--you are--\xe2\x80\x99 The man\xe2\x80\x99s eyes closed, as if the effort at\r\nrecollection exhausted him. He kept them closed as he continued to\r\nspeak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know who you are. You are--the gentleman.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, that\xe2\x80\x99s it, I\xe2\x80\x99m the gentleman,--name of Atherton.--Miss Lindon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfriend. He raised the patient\xe2\x80\x99s head,\r\nallowing it to trickle down his throat. The man swallowed it\r\nmechanically, motionless, as if unconscious what it was that he was\r\ndoing. His cheeks flushed, the passing glow of colour caused their\r\ncondition of extraordinary, and, indeed, extravagant attenuation, to\r\nbe more prominent than ever. The doctor laid him back upon the bed,\r\nfeeling his pulse with one hand, while he stood and regarded him in\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\nThen, turning to the Inspector, he said to him in an undertone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you want him to make a statement he\xe2\x80\x99ll have to make it now, he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngoing fast. I\xe2\x80\x99m an Inspector of police, and I want you to tell\r\nme what has brought you into this condition. Has anyone been assaulting\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHolt, opening his eyes, glanced up at the speaker mistily, as if he\r\ncould not see him clearly,--still less understand what it was that he\r\nwas saying. Sydney, stooping over him, endeavoured to explain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The Inspector wants to know how you got here, has anyone been doing\r\nanything to you? Has anyone been hurting you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe man\xe2\x80\x99s eyelids were partially closed. Then they opened wider and\r\nwider. His mouth opened too. On his skeleton features there came a look\r\nof panic fear. He was evidently struggling to speak. At last words came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The beetle!\xe2\x80\x99 He stopped. Then, after an effort, spoke again. \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nbeetle!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s he mean?\xe2\x80\x99 asked the Inspector.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think I understand,\xe2\x80\x99 Sydney answered; then turning again to the man\r\nin the bed. \xe2\x80\x98Yes, I hear what you say,--the beetle. Miss Lindon is disguised in your old clothes,\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,--in my old clothes. My God!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And where is Miss Lindon now?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe man had been speaking with his eyes closed. Now he opened them,\r\nwide; there came into them the former staring horror. He became\r\npossessed by uncontrollable agitation,--half raising himself in bed.\r\nWords came from his quivering lips as if they were only drawn from him\r\nby the force of his anguish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The beetle\xe2\x80\x99s going to kill Miss Lindon.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA momentary paroxysm seemed to shake the very foundations of his being.\r\nHis whole frame quivered. The\r\ndoctor examined him in silence--while we too were still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This time he\xe2\x80\x99s gone for good, there\xe2\x80\x99ll be no conjuring him back again.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI felt a sudden pressure on my arm, and found that Lessingham was\r\nclutching me with probably unconscious violence. I turned to the doctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Doctor, if there is any of that brandy left will you let me have it\r\nfor my friend?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLessingham disposed of the remainder of the \xe2\x80\x98shillings worth.\xe2\x80\x99 I rather\r\nfancy it saved us from a scene.\r\n\r\nThe Inspector was speaking to the woman of the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, Mrs Henderson, perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99ll tell us what all this means. And as for bein\xe2\x80\x99 careful, there\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t no call for you to tell me to be that, for that I always am, as\r\nby now you ought to know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,--I do know. O\xe2\x80\x99 course that ain\xe2\x80\x99t all I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to say,--ain\xe2\x80\x99t I just\r\na-comin\xe2\x80\x99 to it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then come.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you presses me so you\xe2\x80\x99ll muddle of me up, and then if I do \xe2\x80\x99appen\r\nto make a herror, you\xe2\x80\x99ll say I\xe2\x80\x99m a liar, when goodness knows there\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t no more truthful woman not in Limehouse.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWords plainly trembled on the Inspector\xe2\x80\x99s lips,--which he refrained\r\nfrom uttering. Mrs Henderson cast her eyes upwards, as if she sought\r\nfor inspiration from the filthy ceiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So far as I can swear it might \xe2\x80\x99ave been a hour ago, or it might \xe2\x80\x99ave\r\nbeen a hour and a quarter, or it might \xe2\x80\x99ave been a hour and twenty\r\nminutes--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99re not particular as to the seconds.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When I \xe2\x80\x99ears a knockin\xe2\x80\x99 at my front door, and when I comes to open it,\r\nthere was a Harab party, with a great bundle on \xe2\x80\x99is \xe2\x80\x99ead, bigger nor\r\n\xe2\x80\x99isself, and two other parties along with him.
